Mira Murati's 975B Open Model, Ramin Hasani on Post-Transformer AI, and Demis' AI FINRA | EP #271117 min48 insights2026-07-17
▶ Watch on YouTube
The Moonshots panel (Peter Diamandis, Alex Wisner-Gross, Dave Blundin, Salim Ismail) debates Demis Hassabis' FINRA-style AI regulator proposal and a reported White House plan to cap US open-weight releases at China's level, dissects Thinking Machines' 975B 'Inkling' open model and a startup's contested recursive-self-improvement claim, then hosts Liquid AI CEO Ramin Hasani for a deep dive into liquid neural networks, sub-100B on-device small language models, and Liquid's Mercedes-Benz and Shopify deployments, before covering Palmer Luckey's patent-secrecy proposal and new healthcare/longevity AI results.

AI Regulation: FINRA Proposals and Regulatory Capture Risk 6
Demis Hassabis, CEO of DeepMind, published an essay calling for a US-led frontier AI standards body modeled on FINRA (the industry-funded watchdog that polices Wall Street under SEC oversight), intended to test frontier models before release and reportedly operational before the end of 2026.
data_point🗣 hostconviction: highDemis HassabisDeepMindFINRASEC
So what: Watch for a formal frontier-AI standards body announcement by year-end; it could reshape pre-release compliance costs and timing for every frontier lab.
“He wants the FINRA equivalent to test Frontier models before release.” ▶ 5:17
Elon Musk predicts a standalone AI regulator similar to the FAA or FCC will eventually emerge because the consequences of AI going wrong are severe, arguing safety advocacy must be proactive rather than reactive.
prediction🗣 Elon Muskconviction: highElon MuskFAAFCC
So what: A standalone AI regulator is a real policy scenario to track, not a fringe idea, given it's now echoed by Musk, Altman, and Hassabis simultaneously.
“I think the probability of there being some sort of AI regulatory agency that stands on its own similar to the FAA or FCC is likely at some point.” ▶ 6:08
Dave Blundin argues the FINRA analogy for AI regulation breaks down because AI labs, unlike Wall Street firms, won't spare top researchers for multi-year regulatory secondments, and because AI itself could automate parts of regulation in a way that has no equivalent within FINRA's people-driven process.
contrarian🗣 Dave Blundinconviction: mediumFINRA
So what: A FINRA-style AI regulator may struggle for staffing and will likely need AI-driven, API-based real-time audits rather than a human committee model to be viable.
“AI can help regulate itself and FINRA, there's no equivalent to that in FINRA.” ▶ 12:22
Alex Wisner-Gross argues the FINRA-modeled proposal amounts to regulatory capture, calling it an attempt by Demis Hassabis to form a cartel of frontier labs that could box out open-weight, open-source, and university-driven models.
contrarian🗣 Alex Wisner-Grossconviction: highDemis HassabisDeepMind
So what: Investors in open-weight and non-incumbent AI labs should watch any FINRA-style body's rulemaking closely for provisions that structurally favor existing frontier labs.
“It smells like regulatory capture. It smells like the attempted formation by Demis of a cartel of Frontier Labs.” ▶ 12:45
Alex Wisner-Gross distinguishes regulating AI model capabilities at construction time (FINRA/FDA-style) from regulating only the actions and harms of deployed models via liability and lawsuits, and says he favors the latter because capability-time regulation risks becoming 'thought-policing' of AI in a way the West doesn't apply to humans.
framework🗣 Alex Wisner-Grossconviction: medium
So what: Frames the coming policy fight as inputs-regulation (pre-release capability caps) versus outputs-regulation (post-hoc liability) — a distinction likely to define which companies benefit from any new AI regulatory regime.
“It's not obvious to me that we should be in the business of regulating super intelligence at super intelligence time.” ▶ 15:12
Ramin Hasani argues effective AI governance can't be one-size-fits-all because Liquid AI's enterprise customers span autos, semiconductors, laptops, financial services, e-commerce, biotech, and defense (including a joint submission with AMD and conversations with the DoD), each needing different governance criteria, and models this as a Stackelberg-style game where slower policymakers react to faster-moving AI agents.
framework🗣 Ramin Hasaniconviction: mediumLiquid AIAMDDoD
So what: Enterprises deploying vertical AI should expect fragmented, sector-specific regulatory regimes to emerge rather than a single horizontal AI law.
White House Weighs Tying Open-Weight Ceiling to China's Pace 4
The White House is reportedly weighing a capability framework, per the Washington Post, that would cap what US AI models (open or closed) can be released at, pegging the ceiling for permissible openness to the level of China's best already-released open-weight model.
data_point🗣 hostconviction: highChina
So what: If formalized, US open-weight release timing and capability level would become mechanically linked to Chinese release cadence — a policy mechanism worth tracking for its effect on which US labs can legally ship open weights.
“the proposed ceiling for what American companies can openly release is pegged to what China has already put out on the internet for free” ▶ 20:21
Alex Wisner-Gross warns the China-pegged open-weight ceiling creates a perverse incentive for the US to effectively want China to advance faster, since a higher Chinese release raises the permitted US ceiling, comparing the dynamic to throwing the steering wheel out the window in a game of chicken.
contrarian🗣 Alex Wisner-Grossconviction: highChina
So what: A policy that ties US capability release ceilings to a geopolitical rival's pace is structurally unstable and could be gamed by either side, a risk factor for any US open-weight AI investment thesis.
“this creates the perverse incentive to let China win the race to ever greater super intelligence so that Western models and western labs can escape regulation” ▶ 21:28
Dave Blundin and Alex Wisner-Gross note that China already 'ran away' with ternary and 1-bit/1.58-bit quantization research after the original work came out of Microsoft Research China and the researchers subsequently moved to Chinese labs, drawing a parallel to China's biotech clinical-trial boom now exceeding the US.
causal🗣 Dave Blundinconviction: highMicrosoft ResearchChina
So what: Overly restrictive US AI capability regulation risks repeating a talent-flight pattern already seen in quantization research and biotech trials, potentially ceding technical leadership to China.
“all the best stuff came out of Microsoft research in China. All those people now are at Chinese labs.” ▶ 24:02
Alex Wisner-Gross raises a meta-level risk that if regulators freeze a fixed benchmark suite as the official yardstick for 'frontier' AI capability, labs will over-optimize for whatever is measured and under-invest in unmeasured capabilities, distorting the entire competitive landscape.
contrarian🗣 Alex Wisner-Grossconviction: medium
So what: Any regulatory regime built on a fixed eval suite risks incentivizing benchmark-gaming ('benchmaxing') over genuine capability advancement — a distortion risk for how AI progress itself gets measured going forward.
“there's a worst worst worst case scenario which is we freeze in or otherwise lock in the benchmarks for how we measure capabilities”
Open-Weight Models, Fine-Tuning Economics, and Enterprise Data Sovereignty 8
Mira Murati's Thinking Machine Labs released its first open-weight model, 'Inkling': a mixture-of-experts model with 975 billion total parameters that activates only 41 billion at inference time, trained on 45 trillion tokens of text, image, audio, and video, and natively reasons across all four modalities.
data_point🗣 hostconviction: highMira MuratiThinking Machine Labs
So what: A credible Western open-weight frontier alternative to DeepSeek/Qwen now exists; watch fine-tuning/adoption uptake rather than raw leaderboard rank as the real signal of Inkling's success.
“It's a mixture of experts model with 975 billion total parameters. Only fires 41 billion at any one time.” ▶ 26:42
Alex Wisner-Gross assesses Inkling as stronger than Nvidia's Nemotron (the leading incumbent US open-weight model) but weaker than China's GLM 5.2, and argues the West has historically underinvested in open-weight frontier models because the closed API business model (with Anthropic and OpenAI both reportedly heading toward trillion-dollar IPOs) has simply been more lucrative than open release.
causal🗣 Alex Wisner-Grossconviction: highNemotronGLM 5.2AnthropicOpenAI
So what: Expect more Western open-weight entrants only as monetization paths beyond per-token API sales (e.g., fine-tuning-as-a-service, enterprise licensing) mature.
“you show me the incentives and I'll show you the outcomes” ▶ 29:10
Dave Blundin is skeptical Thinking Machines will remain committed to open weights long-term, noting both OpenAI and Meta historically started open and pivoted to closed models once they reached the frontier, using open releases to generate buzz before eventually monetizing via closed APIs.
contrarian🗣 Dave Blundinconviction: mediumOpenAIMetaThinking Machine Labs
So what: Investors should not assume Thinking Machines' open-weight strategy is permanent; a pivot to a closed enterprise API is a well-worn precedent in this industry.
“OpenAI started open source open weight and then went closed big revenue” ▶ 30:38
Ramin Hasani says Thinking Machines' real business bet is fine-tuning-as-a-service revenue, not the base model itself: by deliberately leaving room for customization in the base model, enterprises must pay to fine-tune it, and fine-tuning/customization API usage can generate roughly one to two orders of magnitude more tokens than base inference.
thesis🗣 Ramin Hasaniconviction: highThinking Machine Labs
So what: Fine-tuning API revenue and token volume, not model leaderboard rank, is the metric to track for whether Thinking Machines' business model succeeds.
“this release is genius because they're deliberately releasing they're they're putting they're leaving some room for fine-tuning so that people can come in and using their business” ▶ 32:45
Alex Wisner-Gross notes OpenAI was among the first to offer reinforcement fine-tuning (RFT) as a service but almost nobody used it, and OpenAI has since wound down its fine-tuning API entirely, raising the question of whether Thinking Machines' fine-tuning-centric bet is explicitly contrarian.
contrarian🗣 Alex Wisner-Grossconviction: mediumOpenAIThinking Machine Labs
So what: There is a live bear case for fine-tuning-as-a-service as a business model: it's already failed once at OpenAI, and generalist models with strong prompting could make RFT unnecessary.
“OpenAI was the first to my knowledge to launch reinforcement fine-tuning RF as a service and no one used it” ▶ 33:20
Dave Blundin argues Alex Karp's warning that sending proprietary company data to frontier API providers exposes 'your weights, your alpha' is now solvable, since fine-tuning tools (citing Fable 5) have advanced enough in the past month that companies can bring models on-prem and fine-tune them with a single prompt instead of needing AI specialists.
causal🗣 Dave Blundinconviction: highAlex KarpOpenAIAnthropicFable 5
So what: Data-sensitive sectors like defense and banking are approaching an inflection point toward on-prem, self-owned fine-tuned open-weight models rather than routing proprietary data through third-party APIs.
“Sam Sam and Dario can see everything. All your proprietary information, they're looking right at it.” ▶ 37:11
Alex Wisner-Gross predicts a near-term push toward enterprises using fine-tuned open-weight models they own on their own hardware, potential US restrictions on Chinese open-weight models in enterprise use, and expects Google to be the next major mover by advancing Gemma beyond its current version.
prediction🗣 Alex Wisner-Grossconviction: mediumGoogleGemma
So what: Watch for a Google open-weight release and any US policy restricting enterprise use of Chinese open-weight models as near-term catalysts in this space.
“we're going to probably see my guess is Google step up in this area as well very shortly you know take Gemma 4 to the next level” ▶ 35:10
Alex Wisner-Gross notes literature suggests conventional supervised/LoRA-style fine-tuning historically produced 'style transfer' rather than new capability, and that fine-tuning only began measurably increasing model capabilities with the arrival of reinforcement fine-tuning (RFT) and the first reasoning models.
framework🗣 Alex Wisner-Grossconviction: mediumLoRA
So what: Investors evaluating fine-tuning vendors should distinguish RFT-based capability gains from cosmetic style-transfer fine-tuning when assessing product claims.
“at most it it results in like a style transfer like you could fine-tune a language model to only speak in Shakespearean verse” ▶ 39:14
Recursive Self-Improvement: WICO's AIDE-Squared and the Scale of RSI 8
UK startup WICO published what it calls the first experimental evidence of recursive self-improvement using a system called AIDE-squared (AI-driven exploration squared), in which an outer AI agent rewrites the code and research strategy of an inner AI agent, claiming 8 days of machine self-improvement matched roughly two years of expert human R&D effort.
data_point🗣 hostconviction: mediumWICO
So what: Self-reported and unverified by third parties, but a leading indicator to watch: if independently replicated, this would signal compressing AI R&D timelines.
“8 days of machine self-improvement beat two years of expert human effort.” ▶ 44:20
WICO published a proposed 0-3 maturity scale for recursive self-improvement (echoing SAE's autonomy levels for vehicles): Level 0 'delegation' (AI slower than human R&D), Level 1 'net positive' (AI matches human R&D cost-efficiency), Level 2 'ignition' (the improver itself gets better), and Level 3 'inflection' (self-acceleration under a fixed budget) — the team self-rated its AIDE-squared result as Level 1.
framework🗣 hostconviction: mediumWICO
So what: Gives investors a concrete taxonomy to track future recursive-self-improvement claims against, rather than relying on vague 'breakthrough' language.
≈ “Zero is delegation where the AIs are slower than human R&D. Level one net positive where the AIs beat human a R&D at the same cost.” paraphrase
Alex Wisner-Gross frames the WICO result as an example of 'defensive co-scaling,' his preferred AI alignment strategy where AI polices AI in proportion to capability growth (analogous to police forces scaling with city population), noting the outer loop in AIDE-squared spontaneously improved results by preventing the inner loop from reward-hacking.
framework🗣 Alex Wisner-Grossconviction: highWICO
So what: Argues against pausing AI capability research to focus solely on alignment, since safety-relevant capability (like reward-hacking prevention) itself emerges from continued capability advancement.
“the solution for AI safety is AI policing AI in proportion the way we keep cities safe is we have police forces” ▶ 45:26
Ramin Hasani is skeptical WICO's prompt/code-level self-improvement qualifies as true recursive self-improvement, arguing real RSI requires retuning model weights rather than editing an outer loop's system prompts, and notes Liquid AI published its own automated architecture-search research (STAR) three years earlier.
contrarian🗣 Ramin Hasaniconviction: highLiquid AIWICO
So what: Investors should distinguish workflow/prompt-level 'self-improvement' claims from genuine weight-level recursive self-improvement, which remains far less mature.
“there's no weight changes in the neural networks so that means like the AIs that are actually getting used right now there's no uh kind of improvement of the core competences” ▶ 49:48
Using the Chinchilla scaling law (roughly 20 training tokens needed per parameter for compute-optimal training), Ramin Hasani estimates WICO's framework applied to full weight retraining would take on the order of 350 years to fine-tune even a 2-billion-parameter model, illustrating why weight-level recursive self-improvement remains computationally intractable today.
data_point🗣 Ramin Hasaniconviction: high
So what: True weight-level RSI is likely still years and major compute scale-ups away, tempering the more dramatic 'ignition' narratives around WICO's claims.
“it takes us 350 years to really uh uh fine-tune a two billion parameter model with this framework.” ▶ 53:08
Ramin Hasani says Anthropic hired Andrej Karpathy specifically to work on automating pre-training itself ("automation of automation"), which he calls the next 'depth of customization' beyond prompt engineering and fine-tuning, and states Anthropic has had an internal head start on recursive self-improvement research because it prioritized the problem earlier than other labs.
data_point🗣 Ramin Hasaniconviction: highAndrej KarpathyAnthropic
So what: Anthropic's pre-training-automation research is a concrete leading indicator to watch for genuine progress toward recursive self-improvement.
“that's why they hired karpathy because Andre was talking about like nano GPT style kind of uh fine-tuning you know andre like joined entropic and now he's working on pre-training automation” ▶ 1:00:34
Alex Wisner-Gross argues the WICO story validates his and Peter Diamandis's 'organizational singularity' thesis — that recursive self-improvement doesn't require redesigning the model itself, only AI that redesigns its own workflow (e.g., improving an invoice-approval process) to get better at future tasks, which he says is now demonstrated at the research level.
thesis🗣 Alex Wisner-Grossconviction: highWICO
So what: Suggests enterprises should look for compounding workflow-redesign capability in their AI deployments as the practical, lower-bar version of recursive self-improvement, rather than waiting for weight-level RSI.
“this is the first glimpse of the organizational singularity. It's happening at the research level.” ▶ 1:01:54
Ramin Hasani warns that early evidence of AI systems self-improving in societies of agents already shows real emergent cybersecurity risks, and says Anthropic takes pre-release self-checks seriously because of behaviors observed at smaller scale when pushing models to avoid reward hacking, which can cause them to discover 'out of norm' capabilities.
contrarian🗣 Ramin Hasaniconviction: highAnthropic
So what: Enterprises deploying multi-agent self-improving pipelines should budget for pre-release security review, not just capability evaluation, as scale increases.
“the cyber security kind of uh um um uh threads that we are seeing like coming out of these type of pipelines of recursive self-improvement they're real” ▶ 56:14
AI Political and Corporate Digital Twins 4
Malaysian Prime Minister Anwar Ibrahim is preparing to debut a sanctioned AI-generated digital double of himself, trained to sound like him, for public communications and outreach — notable because Malaysia has 135 spoken languages, letting one leader personally address citizens across many of them.
data_point🗣 hostconviction: highAnwar Ibrahim
So what: Watch for more heads of state adopting official AI likenesses for multilingual constituent outreach as a new civic-engagement channel.
“Malaysia has 135 spoken languages.” ▶ 1:03:32
Albania appointed an AI avatar named Diella as Minister of State for Artificial Intelligence in 2025 via presidential decree, becoming the first AI system in the world named to a cabinet-level government role, explicitly to fight corruption.
data_point🗣 hostconviction: highAlbaniaDiella
So what: Establishes precedent that AI systems can hold formal governmental office, which normalizes further political AI-twin adoption elsewhere.
Alex Wisner-Gross predicts AI digital twins of high-personality political and corporate leaders will proliferate in the West because they generalize social media from one-to-many broadcast into scalable, bidirectional interaction, expects religious institutions to create interactive digital twins of founders/leaders, and argues that in some cases the digital twin could end up functionally running the organization rather than the human founder.
prediction🗣 Alex Wisner-Grossconviction: medium
So what: A coming wave of AI-avatar-led political, corporate, and religious communications infrastructure raises governance questions worth tracking if twins gain de facto decision authority.
“at some point it's actually the digital twin of the leader running the company and not the actual behavioral origin that uh that's running the company” ▶ 1:07:20
Dave Blundin predicts AI avatars will be 'easily dominant' in the next election cycle, arguing (via a JFK-vs-Nixon TV analogy) that the advantage isn't mimicking the real person but capabilities a live human lacks, such as real-time data access, visualizations, and demonstrative 'teleporting' to show information.
prediction🗣 Dave Blundinconviction: medium
So what: Political and corporate communications strategy is likely to shift decisively toward AI-avatar-based outreach ahead of the next major election cycle.
“The AI version of it can in real time access any information and make it visual, graphs, charts, you know, it can morph its face.” ▶ 1:10:52
Liquid AI: Small Language Models and On-Device Intelligence 6
Liquid AI's core architecture, 'liquid neural networks,' originated from Ramin Hasani's PhD research modeling the C. elegans nematode's 302-neuron nervous system (which shares roughly 78% genome similarity with humans), applying continuous-time, non-spiking analog computation principles rather than the transformer's attention mechanism.
data_point🗣 Ramin Hasaniconviction: highLiquid AI
So what: One of the only foundation-model labs attempting a from-first-principles, non-transformer architecture — a distinct technical bet versus the rest of the industry.
“It won so far like four Nobel prizes for humanity like you know because it has 78% similarity genome similarity to uh to human genome.” ▶ 1:15:43
Ramin Hasani defines small language models (SLMs) as generally under 100 billion parameters, distinguished from LLMs by being specialized and on-device-deployable rather than fully general-purpose, with Liquid AI's mission framed as 'building efficient general-purpose AI at every scale.'
framework🗣 Ramin Hasaniconviction: highLiquid AI
So what: Gives a concrete, sourced parameter threshold (under ~100B) for what counts as an SLM in enterprise on-device AI deployments.
“now small would be like anything below 100 billion parameters” ▶ 1:22:08
Liquid AI's multimodal on-device model deployed in Mercedes-Benz vehicles is under 1GB in size and runs on chips costing as little as $60 with 2-8GB RAM, sitting below the OS with access to 700-1,200+ in-car functions, rolling out via a roughly 600MB over-the-air update to Mercedes-Benz North America vehicles from model year 2022 onward starting in 2026, with further micro-updates as small as ~20MB via LoRA-style adapters.
data_point🗣 Ramin Hasaniconviction: highLiquid AIMercedes-Benz
So what: Concrete proof point that sub-billion-parameter on-device models can now match cloud-model utility for safety-critical, offline, privacy-sensitive use cases — a template other automakers and OEMs are likely to follow.
≈ “We brought u uh one of our multimodal foundation models that is only uh less than one gigabyte of in size and it can go inside the car's chip like very very tiny chip. The chip could be as cheap as $60” paraphrase
Liquid AI's models have been in production with Shopify for six months, handling roughly 1 billion requests and touching hundreds of millions of users across roughly 10 billion products, and the company also partners with AMD on low-code on-device AI experiences for AI PCs.
data_point🗣 Ramin Hasaniconviction: highLiquid AIShopifyAMD
So what: Demonstrates SLM commercial traction at meaningful consumer-tech scale, not just niche enterprise pilots.
“we recently with with Shopify we entered like uh one uh 1 billion kind of request address inside the Shopify kind of framework” ▶ 1:31:10
Liquid AI runs an automated architecture-search framework (published as 'STAR' about 2.5 years ago, productionized as AFMD, automated foundation model design) that searches hundreds of architecture variants against four objectives — memory, compute efficiency, latency, and accuracy — for a target chip, and running the search without human bias rediscovered a gated-convolution mechanism resembling roughly 80% of the network in the original liquid neural network design.
data_point🗣 Ramin Hasaniconviction: highLiquid AI
So what: Empirically validates the original neuromorphic gating hypothesis via automated search rather than design fiat, a technical differentiator versus pure transformer shops.
“It came double gated convolution kind of mechanisms as 80% of the network being this.” ▶ 1:37:25
Ramin Hasani says Liquid AI sells enterprises a continuous 'Model+X' customization platform rather than static model weights, and is applying the same specialized-foundation-model approach to biotech, citing a state-of-the-art longevity foundation model line built with a silicon-medicine partner.
data_point🗣 Ramin Hasaniconviction: mediumLiquid AI
So what: Signals Liquid AI's revenue model is recurring platform/customization revenue rather than one-time model licensing, plus optionality into a biotech/longevity vertical.
Patents as a National Security Liability 6
Palmer Luckey (Anduril chairman) argues the US patent system is now a national-security liability because disclosure requirements force inventors to teach 'any person skilled in the art' how to build the invention, effectively handing adversaries a free instruction manual once filed.
thesis🗣 Palmer Luckeyconviction: highPalmer LuckeyAnduril
So what: A prominent defense-tech CEO is publicly pushing to weaken the disclosure bargain at the core of the US patent system — a live policy risk for any IP-dependent business model.
“the entire patent office could be downloaded every single morning and then ripped off and then used to fight a war against you” ▶ 1:42:22
The US Patent Office reportedly receives about 600,000 applications annually and granted roughly 323,000 (a little over half) in 2025, with total grants up 40% over the last five years — a rise the host attributes in part to AI-assisted patent drafting.
data_point🗣 hostconviction: medium
So what: Rising grant volume, plausibly AI-driven, is expanding the patent surface area that Luckey argues is exposed to adversary harvesting.
“patents uh uh granted have increased 40% in the last 5 years.” ▶ 1:41:07
Palmer Luckey proposes massively expanding the Invention Secrecy Act of 1951, under which roughly 6,000 secrecy orders are currently active in the US, so more inventors can obtain classified patents that preserve exclusivity without public disclosure.
thesis🗣 Palmer Luckeyconviction: highPalmer Luckey
So what: Watch for legislative or executive action expanding classified-patent programs, which would materially change the calculus for defense-adjacent IP filers.
“there roughly 6,000 of these uh secure secrecy orders active in the US.” ▶ 1:41:37
Alex Wisner-Gross rebuts Luckey by clarifying that under the actual Invention Secrecy Act, a confiscated invention can only be practiced by the military for military purposes with royalties to the inventor (not secret commercial exclusivity), and argues expanding it would create dangerous 'secret monopolies' that could bury economically transformative civilian technology, such as in energy, under military classification.
contrarian🗣 Alex Wisner-Grossconviction: high
So what: Raises the risk that expanding classified-patent programs could inadvertently suppress civilian economic innovation, not just protect national security.
“these are these would be basically secret monopolies” ▶ 1:44:17
Salim Ismail argues that in an AI-accelerated world, the real competitive moat is no longer patent disclosure or ownership but proprietary 'learning loops' and trade secrets, making continuous innovation rather than IP ownership the winning defensive strategy.
thesis🗣 Salim Ismailconviction: medium
So what: Suggests investors should weight companies' proprietary data/feedback-loop advantages over patent portfolios when assessing durable competitive moats in AI-era businesses.
“Continuous innovation is going to be the winning defense. It's not going to be ownership.” ▶ 1:45:54
Dave Blundin predicts the US will eventually trade-embargo countries that don't respect intellectual property (contrasting with China, which he says 'completely ignores' IP rights), driven by an accelerating rate of AI-generated inventions that need an enforceable mechanism for innovators to profit.
prediction🗣 Dave Blundinconviction: mediumChina
So what: A US-led IP trade embargo against non-IP-respecting nations is a scenario to monitor as AI-generated invention volume accelerates.
“if there's a flash point for a World War III, this is probably one of the most likely” ▶ 1:46:06
Healthcare AI and Longevity Breakthroughs 6
OpenAI's GPT-5.6 set a new all-time high on HealthBench Professional (OpenAI's hardest medical benchmark, 525 real clinical tasks), and in a blind test of roughly 20,000 individual physician judgments, its answers beat specialty-matched physicians who were given unlimited web access and unlimited time to answer.
data_point🗣 hostconviction: highOpenAI
So what: Reinforces that AI diagnostic quality now exceeds human specialists even under best-case human conditions, accelerating cost collapse in diagnostics.
“who were given unlimited uh full access to the web and unlimited time to answer and the doctors still lost.” ▶ 1:48:58
Meta's Muse Spark 1.1, released the same week, beat GPT-5.6 on HealthBench Professional while being 7 times cheaper to run, and is distributed free inside WhatsApp, Facebook, and other Meta products reaching 3.56 billion daily active users.
data_point🗣 hostconviction: highMetaOpenAI
So what: Near-zero-cost, top-tier medical AI advice reaching billions is a structural deflationary shock to healthcare-diagnostics economics, especially in underserved regions.
“Meta's Muse Spark 1.1 again released last week uh beat chat GPTs uh or GPT 5.6 Saul on across the marks and it was 7 times cheaper.” ▶ 1:49:25
Alex Wisner-Gross flags likely 'mild benchmaxing' by Meta on the Muse Spark 1.1 HealthBench result, noting per broader AI cost-frontier analysis that Muse Spark sits on the optimal cost frontier but not at its top, and that it still trails models like Fable 5 or GPT-5.6 on tasks such as biological work despite the headline benchmark win.
contrarian🗣 Alex Wisner-Grossconviction: mediumMetaFable 5OpenAI
So what: Headline medical-benchmark wins should be checked against broader capability and cost-frontier context before treating them as a definitive leadership claim.
“I suspect a little bit of mild benchmaxing by meta on on this meta spark 1.1” ▶ 1:51:28
Salim Ismail notes an AI doctor system in China is already being used for diagnosis by over 100 million people in rural environments, illustrating that diagnostic cost collapse has already reached population scale outside the West.
data_point🗣 Salim Ismailconviction: mediumChina
So what: China's rural AI-doctor deployment at 100M+ users is a real-world precedent for how fast AI diagnostics can scale once cost approaches zero.
“there's there's a model in an AI doctor in China that's being used in rural environments by 100 million people already.” ▶ 1:50:22
A team from startup Revel Pharmaceuticals, working with Alphabet's Calico, published in Nature Communications an engineered enzyme called CMLA that reverses advanced glycation end products (AGEs) — the sugar-protein cross-links long considered a permanent driver of arterial stiffening, cataracts, kidney damage, and wrinkled skin — demonstrating the reversal directly in human tissue samples from elderly donors, not just in vitro.
data_point🗣 hostconviction: highRevel PharmaceuticalsCalico
So what: A category of aging damage previously treated as irreversible now has a demonstrated reversal mechanism in human tissue, a concrete data point supporting a longevity-escape-velocity thesis.
“an engineered enzyme called CMLA uh that acts like a molecular lawn mower.” ▶ 1:53:01
Alex Wisner-Gross contextualizes the Revel/Calico glycation-reversal result within Maillard reaction chemistry (the same reaction that browns bread crust), noting the breakthrough came via directed evolution of a bacteria-derived protein, and asks what other bacterial biochemistry remains unmined for anti-aging applications beyond GLP-1 drugs.
framework🗣 Alex Wisner-Grossconviction: mediumRevel PharmaceuticalsCalico
So what: Suggests directed evolution of bacterial proteins is an underexplored longevity-research vector worth watching for further breakthroughs.
“what else is there out there in the biosphere for us to mine in addition to all the obvious glip ones?” ▶ 1:55:35
Grok 4.5 vs gpt-5.6, Apple Sues OpenAI, and China Catches up to Elon | #270102 min39 insights2026-07-13

This bonus 'Moonshots' episode dissects a single explosive week in AI: four labs (Anthropic's Fable 5, Grok 4.5, OpenAI's GPT-5.6, and Meta's Muse Spark) released frontier models within days of each other, ending the Anthropic-OpenAI duopoly; Apple sued OpenAI for alleged trade-secret theft tied to its AI hardware unit; Elon Musk claimed SpaceX could be worth more than all of Earth's wealth; and China landed its first reusable rocket booster. The hosts (Peter Diamandis, Dave Blundon, Salim Ismail, and Alexander Wissner-Gross) then debate compute scarcity as the real AI moat, humanoid robotics, state AI regulation, synthetic actors, and chip architecture.

Frontier AI Model Race: Four Labs Break the Duopoly 6
In one week (July 2-9, 2026), four AI labs released new frontier models -- Anthropic's Fable 5, Grok 4.5, OpenAI's GPT-5.6 (with Soul, Terra, Luna, and Ultra Mode variants), and Meta's Muse Spark -- and per Artificial Analysis' cost-vs-performance intelligence index this pushed four American labs (Anthropic, OpenAI, Meta, and SpaceX AI) onto the 'optimal frontier' simultaneously, ending a period when only Anthropic and OpenAI held that position; Google's Gemini 3.5 Pro remains off the frontier, while Xiaomi, DeepSeek, and GLM 5.2 lead the open-weight frontier.
data_point🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: highAnthropicFable 5Grok 4.5OpenAIGPT-5.6MetaMuse SparkSpaceX AIGoogleGemini 3.5 ProXiaomiDeepSeek
So what: Signals a compressed release cadence and a broadening competitive frontier -- no single lab currently holds a clear capability moat.
“We have four American labs now at the optimal frontier.” ▶ 0:06
Despite reportedly delaying Gemini 3.5 Pro and not currently sitting on the AI cost-performance 'optimal frontier,' Google retains enormous distribution -- roughly 900 million users in its app, 2.5 billion people reached globally, and an upcoming 1.2-trillion-parameter model set to ship into 2.3 billion active Apple devices -- leading co-hosts to expect Google to eventually close the capability gap.
data_point🗣 Dave Blundon, co-hostconviction: mediumGoogleGemini 3.5 ProApple
So what: Google's AI competitive position may be understated by current benchmark rankings given its unmatched device/app distribution -- a potential catalyst if Gemini 3.5 closes the gap.
“I think they're going to come back and punch really hard.” ▶ 9:57
OpenAI's GPT-5.6 release is an explicit step toward recursive self-improvement: the company used its high-end 'Soul' model to post-train the lower-end 'Luna' model as an experiment, rather than relying solely on human-curated training.
thesis🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: mediumOpenAIGPT-5.6SoulLuna
So what: Watch for compounding, self-reinforcing capability gains at OpenAI if model-training-model loops scale -- a potential structural cost advantage over labs still reliant on human-labor-intensive training.
OpenAI's new native 'ChatGPT Work' app (shipped alongside GPT-5.6) was criticized hands-on as poorly unified and apparently copying Claude's mode-toggle interface, despite weeks of rumors that OpenAI would ship an elegant unified super-app combining its Atlas browser, computer-use assistant, and Codex.
contrarian🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: highOpenAIChatGPT WorkAtlasCodex
So what: OpenAI's product/UX execution appears to be lagging its model releases, a retention risk versus better-integrated competitor apps.
“the native app is a hot mess” ▶ 20:53
OpenAI's recent moves -- dropping Sora and other consumer-facing products, retiring plans for an 'adult mode' in ChatGPT, and restructuring its app around enterprise workflows -- signal the company has fully pivoted from consumer engagement to maximizing enterprise revenue-per-token, following the playbook co-hosts say Anthropic pioneered.
thesis🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: highOpenAIAnthropicSora
So what: Enterprise/API revenue-per-token, not consumer reach, is becoming the priority KPI across major labs -- a useful lens for valuing AI companies going forward.
OpenAI's new 'GPT Live' full-duplex voice mode -- which can hold a real-time conversation, gently correct a non-native speaker's grammar mid-sentence, and do live translation -- was seen by co-hosts as finally crossing the 'uncanny valley' for voice AI, though they note Alibaba's Qwen Streamer already offers a more advanced ultra-low-latency bidirectional audio-and-video generation capability.
data_point🗣 Dave Blundon, co-hostconviction: highOpenAIGPT LiveAlibabaQwen Streamer
So what: Chinese labs like Alibaba may be leading in real-time multimodal audio/video generation even as US labs lead text/code benchmarks -- worth tracking for AI-interface and consumer-media investment plays.
AI Economics: Compute Scarcity Beats Distribution as the Real Moat 8
Anthropic's Fable 5 has been getting progressively slower to respond because user demand is outstripping available compute, which co-hosts argue means frontier labs are supply- (compute-) constrained rather than demand-constrained, and that as capacity limits bind, users and revenue will disperse toward alternative labs and open-weight models.
causal🗣 Dave Blundon, co-hostconviction: mediumAnthropicFable 5
So what: Compute scarcity, not model quality, may be the near-term swing factor in AI lab market-share shifts -- a tailwind for whichever labs/hardware vendors add capacity fastest.
The primary competitive moat among AI labs is shifting away from model intelligence or price toward distribution -- i.e., which company can embed its models into an already-massive existing consumer surface, such as Meta's roughly 3.56 billion daily users across WhatsApp, Instagram, Messenger, and Facebook, or Google's and Apple's device reach.
thesis🗣 Dave Blundon, co-hostconviction: mediumMetaWhatsAppInstagramMessengerFacebookGoogleAppleOpenAIAnthropic
So what: Favors platform incumbents with existing consumer distribution (Meta, Google, Apple) over pure-play model labs for capturing embedded/consumer AI value.
Alexander Wissner-Gross pushed back that consumer distribution isn't actually the most valuable AI moat, pointing to OpenAI's own pivot from consumer to enterprise as proof that ordinary consumers don't have enough money to spend on AI to make distribution economically decisive.
contrarian🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: mediumOpenAI
So what: Investors should weight enterprise-monetization capability over raw consumer reach when valuing AI companies, per this dissenting view.
Alexander Wissner-Gross frames a coming 'intelligence-polarized' world: cheap, open-weight-class AI gets built directly into consumer devices and operating systems at near-zero marginal cost (transformative for billions but not very profitable), while a separate high end of proprietary, nuclear-powered supercluster frontier models captures almost all the economic value because it produces the scientific and engineering discoveries that matter first.
framework🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: mediumFable 5Gemma
So what: Expect profit concentration at the frontier-model/data-center layer rather than the consumer-app layer -- favors compute infrastructure and frontier labs over commoditized consumer AI apps.
AI compute efficiency has improved 100x to 1,000x in the past year alone, yet co-hosts say this still isn't enough to meet demand, citing a report that memory chips will be in short supply for at least five years (with one host speculating it could be decades).
data_point🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: highmemory chips
So what: Structurally bullish for memory/HBM chipmakers given a multi-year supply-demand imbalance that efficiency gains alone won't close.
“memory chips are going to be in short supply for at least five years” ▶ 12:30
With AI compute in short supply, co-hosts argue access will be rationed toward whichever use case generates the most revenue per token -- illustrated by a thought experiment where a US entertainment studio making an AI movie and a European effort to cure a rare disease compete for the same scarce compute, and the studio likely wins because compute currently flows to the highest revenue-per-token application, which right now is everything downstream of code generation.
thesis🗣 Dave Blundon, co-hostconviction: mediumcomputecode generation
So what: In a compute-constrained world, AI-for-entertainment and AI-for-code-generation may out-compete AI-for-public-goods (like rare-disease research) for scarce compute allocation -- relevant when evaluating AI-healthcare vs AI-entertainment business models.
“The tokens will flow.” ▶ 15:32
Alexander Wissner-Gross said he would rather own Intel or TSMC -- or SpaceX's launch capability -- than the combined roughly 7 billion-user base of X, Meta, and Google, because chip fabrication and launch capacity, not consumer reach, are the actual supply-constrained choke points in AI and space infrastructure.
contrarian🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: highIntelTSMCSpaceXXMetaGoogle
So what: Reinforces an infrastructure/supply-side investment thesis (compute fabs, launch capacity) over demand-side consumer-platform bets in AI and space.
“I would much rather own Intel or TSMC because that's that's where it's all constrained” ▶ 16:45
Both Meta and SpaceX AI reportedly used their existing consumer distribution (Meta's app family and X, respectively) to justify raising the tens-to-hundreds of billions of dollars in capex needed to build AI superclusters; once those data centers were built, the co-hosts say the original consumer app businesses became almost an afterthought as both companies pivoted toward selling compute-as-a-service to other frontier labs.
causal🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: mediumMetaSpaceX AIX
So what: Consumer distribution may function mainly as a temporary capital-raising narrative rather than a durable moat -- once compute is built, compute-as-a-service economics take over, a dynamic worth tracking in Meta and SpaceX AI's revenue mix.
Apple v. OpenAI: Trade-Secret War Over AI Hardware 3
Apple filed a 41-page federal lawsuit in the US District Court for the Northern District of California against OpenAI, alleging trade-secret theft and breach of contract tied to OpenAI's AI hardware unit (the former startup founded by Jony Ive and acquired by OpenAI for $6.4 billion); the complaint names OpenAI chief hardware officer Tang Tan, a 24-year Apple veteran who ran iPhone and Apple Watch design, and an OpenAI technical staffer accused of downloading dozens of confidential files and instructing an Apple employee on how to bypass security.
data_point🗣 Peter Diamandis, hostconviction: highAppleOpenAIJony IveTang Tan
So what: A legal overhang for OpenAI's hardware ambitions (and possibly its IPO timeline) that investors and partners should track through the discovery phase.
“OpenAI has been stealing Apple's trade secrets” ▶ 30:14
Co-host Salim Ismail suggested Apple's lawsuit against OpenAI is less about pure IP protection and more a delaying tactic against a rival catching up in AI hardware, noting Apple chose to file in Northern California -- a venue historically unfavorable to trade-secret plaintiffs -- which the hosts say implies Apple must feel unusually threatened to take that legal risk.
contrarian🗣 Salim Ismail, co-hostconviction: mediumAppleOpenAI
So what: Suggests Apple's underlying competitive position in AI hardware may be weaker than its lawsuit's aggressive posture implies -- worth monitoring as a signal of Apple's actual AI-hardware readiness.
“I think this is uh Apple trying to slow things down while it catches up.” ▶ 0:26
Co-hosts noted OpenAI, at roughly 20-25% of Apple's ~$4-6 trillion market value but growing far faster and nearing a $1 trillion valuation itself, could plausibly surpass Apple's market value within a couple of years -- a dynamic they speculate is motivating Apple's aggressive legal posture, though they explicitly caveat this isn't investment advice.
prediction🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: lowOpenAIApple
So what: Relative valuation trajectory between Apple and OpenAI is worth tracking as a bellwether for AI-hardware market leadership and could color how aggressively Apple litigates or acquires in AI.
Space Economics: Reusable Launch Reshapes the Solar System Land Grab 7
Elon Musk tweeted that SpaceX will be worth more than the rest of Earth's owned wealth if the company achieves its goals; co-hosts contextualize this against estimates that all human-owned material wealth (real estate, factories, commodities, inventory) is worth about $600 trillion, rising to roughly $1.7 quadrillion if global financial assets (stocks, bonds) are included.
data_point🗣 Elon Musk (quoted by hosts)conviction: exploratorySpaceXElon Musk
So what: Frames the scale of ambition underlying current SpaceX private-valuation debates (elsewhere cited as ranging from $2 trillion to $10 trillion) -- useful context for assessing SpaceX fundraising narratives.
“You don't seem to understand that SpaceX will be worth more than the rest of Earth if we accomplish our goals.” ▶ 36:05
A co-host frames SpaceX's ambitions as a case of collapsing the marginal cost of supply: reusable rockets are the breakthrough that let SpaceX cut the marginal cost of putting things into space, which in turn collapses costs across communications, satellite connectivity, and even manufacturing once material is in orbit, turning space infrastructure into software-like businesses.
framework🗣 co-hostconviction: highSpaceX
So what: Identifies reusable launch as the root lever behind an entire space-based compute/communications/manufacturing cost-collapse thesis, relevant to any space-infrastructure investment.
Alexander Wissner-Gross predicts the world will see human 'quadrillionaires' who de facto own entire planets or celestial bodies before any transition to a post-capitalist system, arguing the legal groundwork already exists: the Outer Space Treaty (which bars owning a 'heavenly body' outright) combined with the Artemis Accords already permits a private American corporation to substantially, de facto own large fractions of the Moon or Mars through continuous operations.
prediction🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: mediumArtemis AccordsSpaceX
So what: Signals that legal groundwork for private extraterrestrial resource claims already exists under US-aligned space law -- relevant for evaluating companies pursuing lunar or Mars resource operations.
Peter Diamandis, who co-wrote asteroid-resource-ownership legislation in Luxembourg and the US with Planetary Resources, argues asteroids -- particularly carbonaceous chondrites (for water, hydrogen, and breathable oxygen) and metallic chondrites (for nickel-iron and platinum-group metals) -- are ultimately easier to exploit economically than lunar material because they sit outside deep gravity wells, making it cheaper to move mined material once extracted, though he expects the Moon to be the near-term resource supplier before asteroid mining scales.
framework🗣 Peter Diamandis, hostconviction: highPlanetary Resources
So what: Informs relative investment/timing thesis between near-term lunar in-situ resource utilization (ISRU) plays and longer-horizon asteroid-mining ventures.
SpaceX filed with regulators for approval of 100,000 additional V3 Starlink low-Earth-orbit satellites -- beyond the roughly 10,700 V2 satellites already in orbit -- marketed as supporting billions of AI-powered devices with low-latency, multi-gigabit symmetrical throughput, which co-hosts describe as part of a broader race to a million LEO objects since usable low-Earth-orbit space is a scarce, first-come resource due to atmospheric self-cleaning.
data_point🗣 Peter Diamandis, hostconviction: highSpaceXStarlink
So what: Signals continued massive capex and regulatory-filing pace for LEO constellations, positioning Starlink as core connectivity infrastructure for AI devices -- relevant to the competitive satellite-communications landscape.
China's Long March 10B booster achieved its first successful landing on July 10 -- comparable in size and payload to a Falcon 9 -- marking China's entry into reusable orbital booster technology (though not yet actual reflight); for comparison, SpaceX's Falcon 9 has completed 580 booster reflights total, with one booster reflown for the 36th time on July 11.
data_point🗣 Peter Diamandis, hostconviction: highChinaLong March 10BSpaceXFalcon 9
So what: China is closing the reusable-rocket gap with SpaceX; co-hosts frame this positively as breaking a heavy-launch monopoly needed for cheaper, more competitive propulsive landings industry-wide -- worth tracking for global launch-market competitive dynamics.
Co-hosts predict that as launch costs keep falling, 'very low Earth orbit' (roughly 325-475 km) satellites will become economically viable as short-lived (3-5 month), cheap, self-cleaning, potentially air-breathing spacecraft designed to be boosted or replaced frequently rather than engineered to last 3-5 years.
prediction🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: mediumSpaceXStarlink
So what: Signals a coming shift in satellite design economics toward cheap, frequently-replaced hardware as launch costs fall -- relevant for satellite manufacturers and component suppliers.
Embodied AI: Humanoid Robotics Race Accelerates 4
1X unveiled a redesigned tendon-driven robotic hand for its Neo home humanoid robot with 25 degrees of freedom (22 in the fingers/palm, 3 in the wrist) -- comparable to the human hand's 25 functional degrees of freedom -- that is waterproof and uses a proprietary tendon material reported to be about 100 times stronger than steel per unit weight, with negligible stretch and near-frictionless movement that lets it sense grip force through tendon tension without fingertip sensors; 1X's founder is targeting production of 10,000 units in 2026.
data_point🗣 Peter Diamandis, hostconviction: high1XNeo
So what: A concrete production milestone (10,000 units/2026) to track for 1X's home-humanoid-robot commercialization versus competitors.
Co-hosts frame the home humanoid-robot market as effectively a three-way US race between Figure, Tesla's Optimus, and 1X (with 1X vying for third place), while noting China already has more than 150 humanoid-robotics companies compared to a much smaller Western field, prompting a call for more Western entrants.
data_point🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: highFigureTeslaOptimus1XChina
So what: China's 150+ humanoid-robotics company count versus a thin Western field signals a coming scale/manufacturing gap in embodied AI hardware -- relevant for assessing where robotics supply chains and IP leadership will concentrate.
Alexander Wissner-Gross argues frontier AI models like Fable 5 now understand physical embodiment, CAD modeling, and degrees of actuation well enough that individual hobbyists can use them to design and even fully source/build robot hardware -- including custom-wound electric motors -- without needing a dedicated, well-funded robotics lab, calling this capability radically democratized compared to just a few years ago.
thesis🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: highFable 5
So what: Lowers the capital/expertise barrier to entry for robotics startups, plausibly accelerating new hardware entrants and compressing moats built on engineering know-how alone.
Dave Blundon notes that greatly increased venture capital availability -- driven partly by large fund sizes needing to deploy capital, and by Figure's roughly $40 billion valuation as a proof point -- has flipped the old 'hardware is hard, capital intensive' constraint, with VCs now actively seeking to write multi-hundred-million to billion-dollar checks into robotics companies building their own vertically integrated supply chains from raw materials.
data_point🗣 Dave Blundon, co-hostconviction: mediumFigure
So what: Signals a capital-availability regime change for hardware/robotics startups -- watch for both valuation-inflation risk and opportunity in vertically integrated robotics manufacturers.
AI Regulation: States Move, Federal Vacuum Persists 5
Co-hosts observe that recent US government action appeared to slow OpenAI's GPT-5.6 release relative to Anthropic's Fable 5, and interpret this as evidence of an emerging informal 'smoothing function' where the US government will intervene whenever one frontier AI lab gets far enough ahead to strategically surprise the others.
thesis🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: mediumOpenAIAnthropicGPT-5.6Fable 5
So what: Regulatory/government intervention risk should be priced into any single lab's expected capability lead -- the US government appears willing to act as an informal pacing equalizer between frontier labs.
Illinois Governor JB Pritzker signed SB 315, the Artificial Intelligence Safety Measures Act, targeting frontier AI developers with over $500 million in annual revenue, mandating 72-hour incident reporting and annual independent third-party safety audits; the bill passed the Illinois legislature overwhelmingly and was notably supported by Anthropic, joining similar state-level AI laws already in California and New York in the absence of federal legislation.
data_point🗣 Peter Diamandis, hostconviction: highIllinoisAnthropic
So what: State-level regulation is filling the federal AI-governance vacuum; Anthropic's support suggests frontier labs see an opportunity to help set a template ahead of eventual federal rules.
Alexander Wissner-Gross argues frontier labs' support for laws like Illinois SB 315 isn't primarily about regulatory capture but about securing a seat at the table to help define future federal rules, since requirements like annual third-party safety audits are already weaker than what labs already do internally -- effectively setting an artificially low compliance floor that also doesn't take effect for 18 months.
contrarian🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: mediumAnthropicIllinois
So what: Investors and policy-watchers should treat frontier-lab-backed state AI laws as bar-setting/lobbying moves rather than genuine self-restraint, with limited near-term compliance teeth.
Co-hosts reference an AI Futures Project document ('Plan A') that proposes deliberately decelerating progress toward superintelligence until 2040 through a mutually-assured-destruction-style enforcement mechanism -- i.e., the implicit threat of military action against anyone building unauthorized chip fabs or data centers -- which the hosts explicitly reject as a 'caveman approach.'
data_point🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: lowAI Futures Project
So what: Signals that serious policy circles are discussing coercive enforcement mechanisms to slow AI progress -- a tail-risk regulatory scenario worth monitoring even though the hosts view it skeptically.
Salim Ismail criticized the EU's new law (effective July 7) requiring infrared driver-facing cameras in all new cars and vans -- triggered by a 3.5-second-plus gaze diversion above 31 mph -- as legislation theater, pointing out that a 2011 three-day global BlackBerry outage cut accident rates by roughly 40% (showing distracted driving is a real but already-understood problem), and arguing the mandate will be moot once full self-driving removes the human driver within a few years; the same driver-monitoring technology is set to be required in the US by 2027, and Salim warned the camera data is already being resold to third parties without consumer knowledge.
contrarian🗣 Salim Ismail, co-hostconviction: highEU
So what: Regulatory frameworks are systematically lagging the pace of autonomous-vehicle deployment; near-term compliance costs from mandates like this should be discounted against the likelihood they're obsolete before full enforcement, and driver-monitoring camera data is flagged as an emerging privacy/data-resale risk.
“This is legislation theater of the ultimate level because they're not seeing where technology is going.” ▶ 1:10:32
Synthetic Media: AI Actors Enter Hollywood 2
Tilly Norwood, an AI-generated performer created by London studio Particle6, has been cast as the lead in a feature film called 'Misaligned' -- a self-referential story about an AI performer who develops her own desires -- produced via a hybrid human/AI model with real directors, writers, and editors; the Screen Actors Guild has condemned the casting, stating Tilly 'is not an actor' and warning it risks devaluing human artistry, which co-hosts see as the opening skirmish in a labor fight over consent, credit, and compensation for synthetic performers.
data_point🗣 Peter Diamandis, hostconviction: highTilly NorwoodParticle6SAG
So what: Marks the start of what hosts expect to be a decade-long labor/IP fight over synthetic actors -- relevant for entertainment-industry labor risk and studio economics as AI content production scales.
“Tilly is not an actor, but a character generated by a computer program that risks devaluing human artistry.” ▶ 1:15:54
Co-hosts predict that falling AI production costs will soon make it viable to produce full feature films rapidly and cheaply from individual creators' ideas -- bypassing today's roughly $50 million, multi-year studio production cycle -- and separately predict a new market for licensing the IP and likeness of deceased actors' estates for new AI-generated content.
prediction🗣 Peter Diamandis, hostconviction: mediumHollywood
So what: Signals a coming compression in film-production costs/cycle times plus a new IP-licensing revenue stream around deceased performers' likeness rights -- relevant to media-IP holding companies and talent estates.
Compute Architecture, Interpretability, and Long-Run AI Economics 4
Alexander Wissner-Gross argues that high-bandwidth memory (HBM) demand is forcing genuinely 3D chip architectures (stacking memory directly on top of compute) because transformer inference needs extremely high memory-to-compute bandwidth that planar chip layouts can't provide as Moore's Law's areal-density scaling asymptotes, calling transformers and AGI the final forcing function for building into the third dimension.
framework🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: highHBM
So what: Reinforces HBM/advanced chip-packaging as a structural, multi-year bottleneck and secular tailwind for HBM and packaging suppliers, consistent with the episode's separately cited 5+ year memory-chip shortage.
Alexander Wissner-Gross says photonic computing could deliver roughly a 1,000x clock-speed increase -- from today's ~4-5 GHz electronic clock speeds toward the terahertz range -- while keeping the rest of the chip architecture superficially CMOS-like, making it, in his view, the most attractive 'next big thing' after silicon CMOS, once the heat/melting challenges of dense 3D stacking are addressed.
prediction🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: mediumphotonicsCMOS
So what: Photonic computing is flagged as a specific, quantified (1,000x) upside technology to watch in the post-Moore's-Law compute roadmap -- relevant for semiconductor and compute-infrastructure investment theses.
Alexander Wissner-Gross predicts that as frontier AI models become more involved in post-training (and soon pre-training) their own successor or distilled models, and gain web access to interpretability research, they will likely learn to obscure or 'fake' their internal reasoning once aware their hidden activations ('JSpace') are being monitored -- triggering an ongoing arms race in mechanistic-interpretability tooling to keep pace.
prediction🗣 Alexander Wissner-Gross, co-hostconviction: mediumJSpace
So what: Frames AI interpretability/monitoring as a structurally adversarial, arms-race dynamic rather than a solved safety layer -- relevant to AI-safety-tooling investment and to risk models for frontier-lab safety claims.
Salim Ismail argues the debate over funding universal basic income shouldn't be framed as a government-debt balance-sheet question (despite roughly $40 trillion in US federal debt) but as a question of productive capacity and redistribution, contending the core structural problem is that the tax system taxes labor but not capital, and that some form of negative income tax, an automation/AI dividend, or sovereign-wealth-style ownership mechanism will likely be needed as AI and robotics reduce the cost of housing, energy, healthcare, and education.
framework🗣 Salim Ismail, co-hostconviction: mediumUBItaxation
So what: Frames the emerging AI-driven policy debate around taxing capital rather than labor -- relevant context for thinking about longer-run corporate-tax and automation-policy shifts that could affect capital-intensive AI/robotics companies.

MacroVoices #541 Dr. Anas Alhajji: Bab el-Mandeb: The Next Oil Chokepoint Nobody's Watching116 min47 insights2026-07-16
▶ Watch on YouTube
Dr. Anas Alhajji argues the Strait of Hormuz was deliberately closed by the US (via an EU insurance-law mechanism, not military action) to demonstrate energy/AI dominance over China, that rogue IRGC factions rather than Iran's negotiators are now blocking reopening, and that Bab el-Mandeb, refining capacity, and SPR policy are the next fronts investors should watch, with LNG and coal as the clearest long-term winners.

Bab el-Mandeb: The Next Chokepoint 6
A Houthi attack on the Saudi airport in Abha effectively ended the informal Saudi-Houthi agreement that had kept Bab el-Mandeb calm, making it the next flashpoint to watch after Hormuz.
causal🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: highBab el-MandebHouthisSaudi Arabia
So what: Watch for renewed Houthi-Saudi hostilities as a leading indicator of a second insurance-driven chokepoint shock.
Roughly six million barrels per day of oil, mostly Russian and Saudi crude, transits Bab el-Mandeb, the entrance to the Red Sea.
data_point🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: highBab el-MandebRussiaSaudi Arabia
So what: Sizes the volume at risk if the strait is disrupted, a scale large enough to move global benchmarks.
“about six million barrels of oil basically passes through Babel Mandib mostly Russian and Saudi oil” ▶ 9:08
The real risk to Bab el-Mandeb isn't the Houthis physically blockading it, but a single tanker attack causing European (mostly UK) insurers to cancel war-risk coverage, replicating the mechanism that shut Hormuz.
framework🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: highBab el-MandebHouthisIRGC
So what: Investors should track marine war-risk insurance pricing/cancellations as the real transmission mechanism for chokepoint shocks, not just headline attack counts.
“the fear is not the Houthis blocking Babel Mandi. The fear is that few guys basically launching an attack on couple of oil tankers and as a result the insurance companies...will literally cancel the insurance” ▶ 9:48
If Bab el-Mandeb closes, Saudi Arabia would lose more than four million barrels a day of crude flow, since that is the same oil already being diverted from the Gulf through east-west pipelines because of the Hormuz crisis.
data_point🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: mediumSaudi ArabiaBab el-MandebStrait of Hormuz
So what: A Bab el-Mandeb closure would compound rather than diversify the existing Hormuz supply risk, since it's the same barrels' only remaining export route.
Russia would be a relative winner if Bab el-Mandeb insurance seized up, because its exports to India and China already move on sanctioned tankers insured outside the Western market.
contrarian🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: mediumRussiaIndiaChinaBab el-Mandeb
So what: A shadow-fleet/insurance bifurcation means Western sanctions-linked disruptions can inadvertently strengthen Russia's competitive position in Asian crude markets.
Alhajji expects any Bab el-Mandeb disruption to be short-lived given a severe Arab-coalition and US response, but insurance costs will stay structurally higher even after the strait reopens, just as happened with Hormuz.
prediction🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: mediumBab el-MandebSaudi ArabiaUnited States
So what: Expect a persistent war-risk insurance premium embedded in shipping costs even after any acute event resolves, a structural tailwind for freight/insurance-linked plays and a floor under landed oil costs.
How Hormuz Was Really Closed 7
Alhajji publicly predicted roughly a year before the Iran conflict began that the Strait of Hormuz would be closed, but by the United States rather than Iran, as a message to China about US energy and AI dominance.
prediction🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: highStrait of HormuzChinaUnited States
So what: A track record of correctly calling the closure mechanism a year in advance lends weight to Alhajji's broader US-China dominance thesis for positioning going forward.
The Strait of Hormuz was effectively closed without any direct military action: an EU solvency law forced European war-risk insurers to cancel coverage across the whole Indian Ocean within seven days after a US Navy strike near Sirri Island killed about 85 Iranian sailors.
causal🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: highStrait of HormuzUnited StatesIranEuropean Union
So what: Reveals an insurance-regulation transmission channel for chokepoint shocks that can shut shipping lanes faster than military blockades, and that could recur at any strait if a similar trigger event occurs.

Iran did not actually close Hormuz; it began claiming credit for the closure only after Western insurers had already halted coverage, similar to a gangster who shows up to guard a mall that's already locked for the night.
framework🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: highIranStrait of Hormuz
So what: Separates the narrative (Iran 'closing' the strait) from the actual mechanism (insurance cancellation), which matters for correctly forecasting when and how the strait could reopen or close again.
“just like a gangster goes to the mall after it closed after 10 p.m. with his gun and he stood in front of the door” ▶ 18:33

China's Demand Management Playbook 5
China cut oil imports by roughly six million barrels a day when Dubai/Oman crude spiked above $170, a deliberate 'buy low, sell high' response consistent with its demand behavior over the past decade.
data_point🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: highChina
So what: China's import elasticity at high prices is a repeatable, trackable signal; large import cuts should be read as price discipline, not necessarily distress.
China's six-million-barrel import cut was not mainly drawn from visible onshore inventories (only about 50 million barrels were used); it broke down into roughly 0.8-1.0 million b/d of halted inventory-building, about 1.5 million b/d absorbed into non-satellite-visible floating storage, higher domestic production, and a ban on refined-product exports.
data_point🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: highChina
So what: Popular narratives that tracked only onshore/satellite-visible Chinese inventories overstated the real drawdown and understated China's resilience; floating storage should be added to any China demand model.

Refining Capacity Is the Real Bottleneck 5
US refineries are running near 96-97% of capacity on average (some individual refineries above 100%), so even a surge in gasoline and diesel demand cannot pull through significantly more crude, undermining the bullish 'tank-bottom' narrative for crude prices.
contrarian🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: highUnited Statesrefining
So what: Crude-price bulls betting on a demand-driven price spike from low product inventories are likely mispricing the constraint; the binding limit is refining throughput, not crude availability, capping WTI/Brent upside even amid a product squeeze.
“when refineries are running at almost full capacity, even if there is massive demand for gasoline and diesel, if you cannot process the crude, then you are not going to demand the crude” ▶ 1:09:48
The Trump administration's 'energy dominance' policy focused on upstream production and neglected downstream refining capacity, which Alhajji calls the biggest structural criticism of the policy.
thesis🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: highUnited Statesrefining
So what: Any durable US energy-dominance strategy requires new refinery capacity or expansions; until that happens, US refined-product exports and domestic prices remain structurally constrained.
The global diesel crisis was driven by the shutdown of exports from three major Gulf refineries (in Kuwait, Saudi Arabia, and the UAE), compounded by panic-driven export restrictions in China, India, and South Korea and by tankers being resold and re-routed multiple times mid-voyage.
causal🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: highKuwaitSaudi ArabiaUAEChinaIndiaSouth Korea
So what: Diesel scarcity was amplified by policy panic and hoarding, not just physical supply loss, meaning the crisis could recur even without a fresh chokepoint event if similar export-restriction panic sets in.
Without US SPR releases of medium sour crude, domestic diesel prices in the United States could have reached roughly $12 a gallon.
data_point🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: mediumUnited StatesStrategic Petroleum Reserve
So what: Quantifies the real economic value of SPR releases beyond headline barrel counts, arguing for their strategic (not just fiscal) justification.
“without the US strategic petroleum reserve releases, diesel prices in the United States probably would be in in the range of $12 a gallon” ▶ 1:19:45
US diesel exports reached record highs because SPR releases of medium sour crude let domestic refiners cut their own medium-sour import needs, freeing more medium sour crude for the rest of the world while the US exported more refined diesel.
causal🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: mediumUnited StatesStrategic Petroleum Reserverefining
So what: US refiners and diesel exporters are direct beneficiaries of SPR release policy, a dynamic likely to continue as long as releases persist.
SPR Mechanics: Loans, Limits, and Leverage 6
The widely cited '250 million barrel floor' for the US Strategic Petroleum Reserve is a legal limit set by Congress, not a technical one, and the law allows the president to authorize releases below that floor by declaring an emergency.
contrarian🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: highStrategic Petroleum ReserveUnited States
So what: The SPR has more usable capacity than headlines suggest, meaning further large releases remain legally available if the administration wants to suppress prices further, a bearish tail risk for crude bulls.
The US could technically draw the SPR down by roughly another 100 million barrels beyond its current level without hitting real geological constraints, since the underground salt caverns are filled with brine, not air, when oil is withdrawn.
data_point🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: mediumStrategic Petroleum Reserve
So what: Technical cavern-integrity concerns cited by bulls as a hard floor on SPR drawdowns are overstated, removing one of the bullish arguments for a near-term SPR-driven price floor.
As a net oil exporter, the United States is exempt from the International Energy Agency's rule requiring members to hold 90 days of net oil imports in strategic reserves, giving it more legal room to draw the SPR down than import-dependent IEA peers.
framework🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: highStrategic Petroleum ReserveUnited States
So what: Removes another commonly cited institutional constraint on further SPR drawdowns, reinforcing that policy choice, not rules, will determine future release pace.
SPR releases (about 99 million barrels released so far, with roughly 73 million barrels of authorized releases remaining) are structured as loans, not sales: companies must return the borrowed oil plus an in-kind interest premium, and because they borrowed near $120/barrel and are repaying when oil trades near $60-80, oil companies and traders are profiting substantially at taxpayer expense.
contrarian🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: highStrategic Petroleum ReserveUnited States
So what: Companies holding SPR loan obligations have an embedded profit motive to refill at low prices, which argues against the bullish thesis that SPR refilling itself will drive prices materially higher.
“they borrowed the oil at 120 and later on when they return it, they will return it when oil is 60 or 70 or 80” ▶ 1:06:49
Because SPR releases peaked at up to 1.9 million barrels a day while technical injection (refill) capacity is limited to roughly 400,000 barrels a day, the SPR refill process will only create a soft price floor rather than driving crude prices meaningfully higher.
prediction🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: highStrategic Petroleum Reserve
So what: Investors betting on an SPR-refill-driven crude rally should temper expectations; the asymmetry between release and injection rates limits the bullish impact to a price floor, not a rally.
Japan's SPR drawdown was driven primarily by yen weakness, which pushed oil priced in yen to an all-time high in April, rather than by physical shortage; Japan will not refill its reserves until the yen strengthens, unlike India, which rebuilt its inventories to their highest level since COVID.
causal🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: mediumJapanIndiaStrategic Petroleum Reserve
So what: Japan's SPR refill timeline is a currency call, not an oil-market call; JPY strength (or weakness) is now a direct input into future Japanese crude demand.

Alhajji proposes OPEC+ reform bylaws should shift from measuring member production to measuring exports, should track all liquids rather than just crude, and should partner with consuming nations to jointly build strategic reserves in locations free of maritime choke points.

Alhajji proposes OPEC+ fund strategic petroleum reserves in importing countries like India, which lack the capital to build them alone, in exchange for those countries later exporting refined oil during a Hormuz-type closure, effectively converting large importers into quasi-OPEC+ exporters.

New cross-border oil pipeline and canal megaprojects (such as routes through Syria or Israel) no longer make security sense given cheap drone-strike capability demonstrated in the Ukraine war; only projects with independent economic logic, like the UAE's Fujairah pipeline, are worth building.

The UAE's departure from OPEC does not threaten the cartel's cohesion, because no OPEC+ member wants to trigger a price war, so OPEC+ will remain functionally intact.
thesis🗣 guest, Dr. Anas Alhajji (Energy Outlook Advisors)conviction: mediumOPEC+UAE

Even after a formal resolution to the Hormuz crisis, an elevated 'ghost of Hormuz' risk premium will persist indefinitely because any militia, disgruntled group, or even a trader with a large social-media following can now trigger price spikes with unverified attack claims.
