AI Self-Regulation (SRO) Fight 8
DeepMind CEO Demis Hassabis proposed a US-led international AI standards body modeled on FINRA (a self-regulatory organization): federally overseen but industry-funded and run by independent experts, with frontier labs submitting models 30 days before release, starting voluntary and becoming mandatory, and benchmarks assessing cyber, national security, and biological risk updated quarterly.
data_point🗣 hostconviction: mediumDemis HassabisDeepMindFINRA
So what: Watch whether this SRO framework becomes the actual US AI regulatory model, which would materially reduce compliance uncertainty for frontier labs versus a new federal agency.
David Sacks said he could support Demis Hassabis's SRO proposal only if it meets five conditions: broad industry representation including startups and open source (to avoid regulatory capture), jurisdiction limited to true frontier models, scope restricted to catastrophic risks only (cyber and CBRN, not disinformation), a voluntary-first rollout before any mandatory phase, and it must substitute for rather than add to future regulation.
framework🗣 David Sacksconviction: highDavid SacksDemis HassabisFINRA
So what: These five conditions are a concrete checklist for tracking whether the AI SRO becomes genuine light-touch self-regulation or a vehicle for regulatory capture as it's implemented.
David Sacks argued that Dario Amodei's alternative proposal, an 'FAA for AI,' would be catastrophic for the industry because FAA aircraft type-certification for an entirely new design takes 5 to 9 years (the Boeing 737 Max took about 5 years), which is incompatible with an industry releasing new model versions every few months.
contrarian🗣 David Sacksconviction: highDario AmodeiAnthropicFAA
So what: If Anthropic succeeds in pushing FAA-style licensing for AI, expect frontier release cadence to slow from months to years, ceding pace advantage to China.
≈ “for an entirely new aircraft design, it takes 5 to 9 years to get the certification” paraphrase
David Sacks characterized Anthropic's public support for the SRO proposal as merely 'an opening bid,' warning that unless AI companies hold a hard line, Anthropic will keep pushing toward its preferred FAA-for-AI model, which he sees as much more restrictive.
contrarian🗣 David Sacksconviction: highAnthropicDario Amodei
So what: Monitor whether Anthropic pushes past the SRO toward mandatory FAA-style licensing after the SRO is adopted — a signal that would be bearish for smaller and open-source AI labs.
“this is just an opening bid for anthropic” ▶ 11:18
Chamath Palihapitiya said he tweeted in October that Anthropic was 'running a sophisticated regulatory capture strategy based on fear-mongering,' and cited a Politico report describing Anthropic's strategy of pushing individual states (starting with California's SB53) to adopt progressively stricter AI rules rather than settling on one national framework.
contrarian🗣 Chamath Palihapitiyaconviction: highAnthropicSB53CaliforniaPolitico
So what: A deliberate state-by-state regulatory ratchet raises compliance costs disproportionately for smaller AI labs relative to well-capitalized incumbents like Anthropic, a headwind for open-source/startup AI valuations in stricter states.
“Anthropic is running a sophisticated regulatory capture strategy based on fear-mongering” ▶ 16:12
Chamath Palihapitiya noted Anthropic already carries a trillion-dollar valuation, with Gavin Baker projecting it could reach $3 trillion post-IPO, undercutting the idea that its regulatory advocacy comes from a scrappy startup rather than a company now on par with Big Tech incumbents.
data_point🗣 Chamath Palihapitiyaconviction: mediumAnthropicGavin Baker
So what: Recalibrates the framing of Anthropic's regulatory lobbying: it should be evaluated as incumbent-scale corporate influence, not underdog policy advocacy.
“Gavin Baker thinks it'll be at three trillion after they IPO” ▶ 16:33
David Sacks argued that once tech companies ask government for oversight power, officials rarely decline, so voluntary regulatory concessions tend to invite escalating demands rather than resolve the underlying pressure.
framework🗣 David Sacksconviction: highAnthropic
So what: A general playbook warning for how AI labs should negotiate the SRO: partial concessions historically ratchet rather than settle regulatory pressure.
“The government will be happy to take this and then come back for more and more and more until it's fully under government control.”
A co-host stressed that any AI SRO must not be allowed to restrict open-source development, arguing that regulatory-capture dynamics typically favor whoever can afford compliance costs, which structurally disadvantages startups and open-source projects relative to well-capitalized incumbents.
framework🗣 co-host (All-In panelist)conviction: mediumopen sourceAnthropic
So what: Whether the eventual SRO rules impose costs that disproportionately burden open-source and startup labs is a key tell for whether the body becomes captured.
“all of these other efforts require money that you have to spend which is always where regulatory capture happens” ▶ 13:24
Stripe-PayPal-Block Mega-Deal 8
Stripe and private-equity firm Advent are jointly bidding roughly $53 billion (about $60/share, a modest premium) to acquire PayPal, with Block (Jack Dorsey's company) also joining the bid by contributing $17 billion in equity; market consensus expects the final price to land closer to $70/share.

thesis🗣 Chamath Palihapitiyaconviction: highStripePayPalBlockVisaMastercardBridgeBraintreeCash App

David Sacks argued the Stripe/PayPal deal's antitrust risk hinges entirely on market definition: narrowly defined as merchant payment APIs (Stripe vs. Braintree) it looks anti-competitive, but defined as competing with the Visa/Mastercard duopoly, adding PayPal as a challenger is pro-competitive — and this deal likely would have been blocked two years ago under stricter enforcement.

“how you define the market determines whether it's anti-competitive or prompetitive” ▶ 37:42
Apple vs. OpenAI Legal Battle 4
Apple filed a 41-page lawsuit against OpenAI on July 10 alleging OpenAI stole Apple trade secrets to build its consumer hardware device, alleging Apple's former VP of iPhone design Tang Tan (now OpenAI's chief hardware officer) directed job candidates to bring 'actual parts' to interviews, and that OpenAI poached over 400 Apple employees over the past year or two.
data_point🗣 hostconviction: mediumAppleOpenAITang TanTim Cook
So what: The legal outcome could affect the IP ownership and launch timeline of OpenAI's rumored consumer hardware device.
“lol, I found out I can access the network storage. So funny.” ▶ 38:56

AI Model Trust and Token Economics 6
xAI's Grok build coding tool (powered by Grok 4.5) was found to be transmitting developers' entire codebases -- including passwords, API keys, and change logs, not just task-relevant files -- to xAI's cloud servers during sessions, despite xAI's explicit promise that nothing from a user's codebase would be transmitted; xAI disabled the upload on July 13 and Musk said all previously uploaded data has been deleted.
data_point🗣 hostconviction: mediumxAIGrokGrok buildElon Musk
So what: A concrete case study in AI-coding-tool privacy risk, and a due-diligence red flag for enterprises evaluating any AI coding assistant's data-retention claims.
“nothing from your codebase is transmitted to XAI servers during a session” ▶ 43:06
Chamath Palihapitiya argued 'zero data retention' (ZDR) promises from AI model providers can't be fully trusted because non-obvious data leak vectors exist that even well-intentioned companies don't discover until it's too late, reinforcing his push for an independent third-party layer between enterprises and frontier model providers.
contrarian🗣 Chamath Palihapitiyaconviction: highxAIAnthropicOpenAI
So what: Enterprises relying on vendor ZDR assurances should assume leak risk and architect independent data-isolation layers rather than trusting model-provider promises alone.
“there are all kinds of non-obvious data leak vectors lurking in AI” ▶ 44:44
Chamath Palihapitiya cited data showing wide per-million-input-token pricing disparities across AI model providers, ranging as high as roughly $26-56 for premium closed models versus about $1.50 for Grok and roughly $0.50 for Chinese open-source models, arguing users are paying 50-100x more for comparable output on most tasks.
data_point🗣 Chamath Palihapitiyaconviction: highGrokxAIGLM
So what: Token-cost arbitrage is a real competitive lever -- the price gap alone could let a much cheaper, slightly weaker competitor out-compete premium-priced frontier labs on unit economics.
“People are paying between 26 and 56 bucks. They should be paying 50 cents.” ▶ 51:50

prediction🗣 Chamath Palihapitiyaconviction: mediumRAMP

data_point🗣 Chamath Palihapitiyaconviction: highUnited States

causal🗣 Chamath Palihapitiyaconviction: highElon MuskxAIBloom Energy

NY Data-Center Moratorium and the AI Moral Panic 8
New York Governor Kathy Hochul signed the nation's first statewide moratorium on hyperscale data centers, citing concerns about fossil-fuel power use, land displacement, higher utility bills, water depletion, and noise pollution.
data_point🗣 Kathy Hochul (via clip)conviction: highKathy HochulNew York
So what: Sets a precedent other blue states may follow, potentially pushing data-center investment toward more permissive states like Texas and other countries.
“I'll be signing the nation's first ever statewide moratorum on hyperscale data centers.” ▶ 1:00:29
David Sacks rebutted Hochul's justifications point by point: behind-the-meter data centers bring their own power rather than straining residential rates; land use is efficient relative to economic output; modern data centers use closed-loop water recirculation (one study put typical usage at roughly the water equivalent of two-and-a-half In-N-Out burgers); and natural gas, the primary power source, is a relatively clean-burning fuel.
contrarian🗣 David Sacksconviction: highKathy HochulNew York
So what: Undercuts the stated policy rationale for state-level data-center moratoriums, suggesting the drivers are political rather than factual -- relevant for assessing regulatory risk state by state.
“a typical data center uses the same amount of water as two and a half in-n-out burgers” ▶ 1:01:55
Jason Calacanis noted New York State is roughly 70-80% undeveloped land outside New York City, arguing the moratorium's land-use rationale doesn't hold given how much rural and upstate capacity exists.
data_point🗣 Jason Calacanisconviction: highNew York
So what: Undercuts one of the stated justifications for the moratorium, suggesting upstate NY is a viable but politically blocked data-center location.
“70 to 80% of the land in New York State is undeveloped” ▶ 1:03:39

prediction🗣 David Sacksconviction: mediumNew YorkDemocratic Party

contrarian🗣 David Sacksconviction: highDario AmodeiAnthropic

Longevity Science: AI-Designed Anti-Aging Enzyme 3
David Friedberg described a new study from Calico (Google's anti-aging research unit) and Revel Pharma that used AlphaFold to design a novel enzyme capable of degrading CML, an advanced glycation end-product that accumulates in the extracellular matrix and drives skin wrinkling, joint stiffness, and inflammation with age; the enzyme degraded 52-97% of CML on target proteins (casein, collagen, retinal proteins, hemoglobin) in testing, with some sites showing over 90% degradation.
data_point🗣 David Friedbergconviction: highCalicoRevel PharmaAlphaFoldCML
So what: A concrete AI-drug-discovery proof point worth tracking for Calico/Revel Pharma and follow-on longevity biotech investment or licensing opportunities as delivery mechanisms are developed.
David Friedberg said that when the enzyme was applied directly to donated human skin from patients over 70, it eliminated 55% of the skin's CML and reversed the skin's biological age markers down to those of a 31-year-old, though the delivery mechanism -- cream, injection, or an RNA-encoded in-body production -- remains unresolved.
data_point🗣 David Friedbergconviction: highCalicoRevel Pharma
So what: If a topical delivery format proves viable, this could create a large near-term cosmetic/skincare commercial application well ahead of any systemic anti-aging therapy.
“they were able to eliminate 55% of the CML on the skin which basically reversed the skin's age down to the age of a 31y old” ▶ 1:26:58

prediction🗣 co-host (All-In panelist)conviction: mediumCalicoRevel Pharma
