“that alone is $2 trillion” ▶ 1:28:23

80,000 Hours Podcast 1 episode(s) · 44 insights

Can $500 Billion Win the AI Race? | Anton Leicht94 min44 insights2026-07-14
▶ Watch on YouTube
Carnegie Endowment fellow Anton Leicht argues that middle powers (Europe, Japan, South Korea, etc.) face a default future of capturing all of frontier AI's risk and none of its benefit unless they pursue a compute-for-access strategy with US labs; he separately sketches, and largely dismisses as unlikely, an allied $500 billion four-year coalition project to build an independent frontier AGI model, while also covering AI taxation, labor-market policy, soft nationalization of US labs, and the pause/slowdown debate.
Middle Powers' Default AI Dependency · 7$500B Coalition Frontier-Model Gambit · 12Taxing and Cushioning the AI Transition · 9Manufacturing as the New AGI Moat · 4Soft Nationalization of US AI Labs · 3Pause, Slowdown, and the China Question · 7Country Winners and Losers in the AGI Economy · 2
Middle Powers' Default AI Dependency 7
Countries that do not build their own frontier AI models default into capturing all the societal risk that comes from these models spreading through society while capturing almost none of the economic benefit, according to Anton Leicht.
framework🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: highmiddle powersfrontier AI models
So what: Frames the baseline policy problem for every non-US, non-China government: passive non-participation in the AI supply chain is itself a strategic loss, not a neutral position.
Leicht's recommended strategy for middle powers to secure reliable AI access is compute-for-access: build data centres that absorb US labs' inference-capacity crunch, and in exchange negotiate guaranteed frontier-model access at parity with the US commercial market.
framework🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: highmiddle powersUS AI labsdata centres
So what: A concrete, testable framework for evaluating national AI deals: watch whether new data-centre buildouts in allied countries come bundled with explicit model-access-parity clauses.
Leicht estimates the realistic benchmark labs need to consider diversifying inference compute outside the US is a data centre built in about 18 to 24 months, ideally offering something like a gigawatt of capacity within one to two years.
data_point🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumdata centresUS AI labs
So what: Gives investors a concrete timeline/scale benchmark for judging whether a given national data-centre project is fast enough to actually earn access-parity leverage with frontier labs.
Direct government-to-government AI deals are fragile: the UK-US Technology Prosperity Deal, which included a Stargate data-centre buildout pillar, unravelled after the US inserted unrelated food-safety demands into negotiations and OpenAI partly reneged on the Stargate component citing copyright and energy-price concerns.
causal🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: highUnited KingdomUnited StatesOpenAIStargate
So what: Warns investors and policymakers that headline US bilateral tech-cooperation deals can be renegotiated or partly abandoned via unrelated political leverage, making them a weak basis for long-term AI-access planning.
Leicht argues deals struck directly with AI labs are more durable than deals struck with the US government, because labs that have sunk billions into a country's data centre gain a commercial incentive to lobby the US government against export restrictions that would strand that investment.
thesis🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumAI labsNvidiaAnthropicUS government
So what: Suggests investors should track lab-to-country commercial commitments (data-centre buildouts, access-parity contracts) as a more reliable predictor of durable AI market access than government treaties.
≈ “if the US government decides to come up with some access restriction, then the American firms that are involved in these deals... now have an incentive to tell the US government... can you at least give a carveout to this country” paraphrase
The UAE's chip-access deal and Norway's original Stargate arrangement (now effectively a Microsoft buildout after OpenAI partly walked back its commitment) both illustrate that early compute deals were struck from weak bargaining positions before frontier-access anxiety became acute, and neither secured genuine access-parity guarantees.
data_point🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumUAENorwayOpenAIMicrosoftStargate
So what: Signals that early-mover AI infrastructure deals in the Gulf and Nordics should not be read as templates for future access-parity negotiations; later entrants may need to negotiate harder terms.
The Fable export-control episode, reported by Wired as tied to Fable's access being extended to South Korea's SK Telecom over alleged China ties, has accelerated Washington's policy conversation toward export-controlling AI models (weights and/or outputs) much faster than Leicht expected, compressing the window middle powers have to set up compute-for-access arrangements.
causal🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: highFableSK TelecomSouth KoreaWired
So what: A concrete near-term catalyst: watch for accelerated US export-control rulemaking on model weights/outputs, which would tighten the timeline for any middle power still negotiating access-parity deals.
≈ “The Fable thing — even if it is a red herring — has shaken up a lot of people, and has made a lot of people more worried about this.” paraphrase
$500B Coalition Frontier-Model Gambit 12
Leicht's own back-of-envelope estimate is that a coalition of middle powers pooling talent, compute, funding, and supply-chain leverage could build a competitive frontier AI model for roughly $500 billion over a four-year project.
data_point🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediummiddle powersfrontier AI model
So what: Sets a concrete, named price tag for an independent non-US/non-China frontier-AI effort that investors and policymakers can use as a reference point when new sovereign AI initiatives are announced.
Leicht argues $500 billion is not an absurd sum if the best version of an allied liberal-democracy coalition — the EU plus Canada, the UK, Australia and Japan and South Korea — pooled resources, since it would be affordable for that combined economic bloc.
thesis🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumEuropean UnionCanadaUnited KingdomAustraliaJapanSouth Korea
So what: Frames the feasibility question as political willingness rather than raw affordability, which is the key variable for investors tracking whether a rival non-US frontier lab could actually get funded.
Leicht's proposed structure for a coalition frontier lab is to replicate the standard frontier-lab organizational model exactly (pay top engineers heavily, give them massive compute, minimal outside interference) rather than reinvent it, funded by irrevocable upfront capital commitments from every member government routed through a small number of designated national liaisons.
framework🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumfrontier AI labscoalition governments
So what: A concrete organizational blueprint that would signal a serious (vs. symbolic) state-backed AI effort if it appeared in real policy proposals.
≈ “You just need to reinvent the wheel... In fact, you mustn’t reinvent the wheel in any of this.” paraphrase
Leicht's preferred coalition membership is the EU, UK, Canada, Australia, New Zealand, South Korea, and Japan (Taiwan if achievable), but he expects the UK, South Korea, and Japan to opt out initially because their current bilateral relationships with the US look like a better bet than joining an EU-led coalition with a poor track record of delivering announced joint projects.
contrarian🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumEuropean UnionUnited KingdomSouth KoreaJapanCanadaAustralia
So what: Predicts a narrower, EU-Canada-Australia-anchored coalition as the realistic starting membership, useful for tracking which countries' policy moves would actually signal this project advancing.
Leicht frames the coalition frontier-model project economically like a warplane or aircraft carrier — a strategic asset governments buy without expecting it to pay for itself — rather than a commercial venture, distinguishing it from US labs which must justify returns to private capital markets that do not exist at scale outside the US.
framework🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumcoalition governmentsUS AI labs
So what: Reframes how to value a state-backed AI project: judge it against defense-budget logic, not venture-style ROI, when assessing whether treasuries will actually fund one.
Leicht is pessimistic that a successful European frontier model would translate into strong international commercial market share, noting Europe has historically found it harder to build and sell a winning software product than to build large infrastructure, even though Mistral retains a decent US market share today.
contrarian🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumMistralEuropean Union
So what: Tempers expectations that a state-funded European frontier model would become commercially self-sustaining or a genuine Nvidia/OpenAI/Anthropic-style competitor even if technically successful.
Leicht's base case is that the $500 billion coalition project does not happen, primarily because political awareness in national treasuries lags the required urgency by months, causing the effective price tag to keep rising — from $500 billion today to an estimated $600 billion in six months, and toward roughly $1 trillion after further delay.
prediction🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: highmiddle powerscoalition governments
So what: A high-conviction call that a state-backed rival to US frontier labs is unlikely near-term; if unexpected coalition funding news emerges, it would materially update this base case.
≈ “So $500 billion is a price tag now. If we convince people to do it, we’re going to have to tell them in six months that now it costs $600 billion.” paraphrase
Leicht's rough estimate for the compute a coalition frontier project would need is on the order of 3 million Blackwell-class chips — more than are currently available to purchase given Nvidia's limited supply — making chip acquisition, not just funding, a binding constraint.
data_point🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumNvidiaBlackwell
So what: Quantifies the hardware bottleneck for any non-US frontier effort; a useful reference point for judging whether announced sovereign AI compute deals are anywhere close to frontier scale.
≈ “something like 3 million Blackwell-class chips for building this thing” paraphrase
Leicht notes much of a frontier lab's effective talent base today consists of coding agents rather than only human engineers, so a coalition project without access to leading tools like Codex or Claude Code would be materially slower to build a competitive model, and the gap would widen the longer that access is denied.
framework🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumcoding agentsCodexClaude Code
So what: Identifies coding-agent access as an underappreciated AI-competitiveness bottleneck alongside chips — worth tracking as a leading indicator of whether any non-US lab can realistically catch up.
≈ “much of the talent base of frontier labs today are already coding agents” paraphrase
Leicht expects Nvidia to be commercially motivated to sell chips broadly to diversify its buyer base, but expects the US government to resist Nvidia selling at scale to a coalition explicitly aiming for frontier parity, since that carries different security optics than routine commercial sales or even sales to China's diffusion-stage buyers.
causal🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumNvidiaUnited StatesChina
So what: Suggests chip-export policy risk for any coalition AI project is asymmetric: Nvidia's commercial incentives favor the deal, but US political/security incentives cut against it, making export-license politics a key catalyst to watch.
Leicht argues the main leverage point for a coalition to secure chips and coding-agent access is the semiconductor supply chain itself — particularly ASML in the Netherlands, plus Japanese and South Korean high-bandwidth-memory and semiconductor-equipment suppliers — since Nvidia and the US depend on these upstream inputs to build chips at all.
framework🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumASMLNetherlandsJapanSouth KoreaNvidia
So what: Identifies ASML and Japanese/Korean semiconductor-equipment suppliers as the practical geopolitical leverage points in any allied AI-access negotiation, relevant for assessing export-control risk around these firms.
Leicht believes US policymakers currently do not take a middle-power government-led AGI project seriously, expecting it to fail like past catch-up projects, which paradoxically means the US may not act early to block chip exports — creating a limited window for a coalition to get chips online before Washington reassesses the threat.
contrarian🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumUnited Statesmiddle powers
So what: Implies a narrow, closing arbitrage window: a credible coalition project might acquire chips precisely because it is currently underestimated in DC, before political attention catches up.
Taxing and Cushioning the AI Transition 9
Leicht argues a US government equity stake in AI companies — an idea associated with Bernie Sanders and reportedly under White House consideration — is a bad policy because even a 10x-100x rise in AI-lab valuations would not come close to funding large-scale social disruption relief, since the broader economy dwarfs the labs even at that scale.
contrarian🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: highBernie SandersUS governmentAI labs
So what: A direct, high-conviction pushback against a live US policy proposal; investors should weight equity-stake-in-AI-labs proposals as economically insufficient even if politically popular.
Leicht warns that a US equity stake tied to AI-lab valuations (e.g., a Trump account funded with AI equity) would create a strong government incentive to under-regulate those labs, since tougher AI regulation would directly reduce the value of the government's own holdings.
causal🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: highUS governmentAI labs
So what: Flags a specific conflict-of-interest mechanism worth monitoring: any formal US government equity stake in frontier AI firms should raise flags about weakened future safety regulation.
For non-US allies, Leicht argues the closest workable analogue to an AI-lab equity stake is a genuinely diversified sovereign wealth fund that includes AI labs as one hedge among many, rather than a narrow fund that only buys stakes in a single AI developer.
framework🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumAnthropicsovereign wealth funds
So what: A concrete design principle for any allied sovereign-AI-investment vehicle: diversification across the AI stack (or broader markets), not concentrated single-company exposure.
≈ “the stupid “I only buy Anthropic stock” version of the sovereign wealth fund” paraphrase
Leicht prefers raising the corporate income tax over a narrow AI-usage 'token tax' (which would blunt AI adoption and competitiveness) or a broad consumption tax (which is politically toxic because it appears as a visible line-item, e.g. on a food-delivery order, even though most of its burden actually falls on companies).
thesis🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumcorporate income taxconsumption tax
So what: Gives a concrete policy preference to watch for: corporate-tax-rate proposals are the more likely funding mechanism for AI-transition safety nets than usage-based AI taxes.
Leicht expects a massive revenue crisis for treasuries broadly as economic activity shifts from heavily taxed human labour toward lightly taxed capital and AI agents, estimating that naively moving one unit of revenue from labour to capital could lose half or more of the associated tax revenue given current corporate tax rates (~25%) and capital investment tax breaks.
data_point🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumcorporate taxlabour markets
So what: A quantified warning about a structural fiscal shock as AI displaces labour income; relevant for anticipating future corporate tax hikes or new capital-taxation regimes across developed economies.
Leicht argues capital-flight risk from AI-heavy firms is more limited than feared because compute-intensive firms remain physically anchored to data centres, meaning a coordinated 10-15% minimum corporate tax floor across allied countries could still capture meaningful revenue despite firms' nominal mobility.
framework🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumdata centrescorporate tax
So what: Suggests physical data-centre location will remain a durable tax-revenue anchor even as firms become more digitally 'mobile,' informing expectations around future minimum-tax coordination among allies.
Leicht makes the counterintuitive recommendation that middle powers should make labour markets more flexible, not more rigid, as AI disruption arrives, because protecting current jobs risks a much worse later outcome (mass layoffs and higher structural unemployment) once protected firms get outcompeted by more AI-adaptive domestic or foreign rivals.
contrarian🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: highlabour marketsmiddle powers
So what: A specific, actionable labor-policy call that runs against the intuitive protectionist response to AI job displacement; useful for evaluating which countries' labor reforms are AI-transition-compatible.
≈ “you have to bite the bullet on the counterintuitive conclusion of making your labour market more flexible as the AI disruption comes in” paraphrase
Leicht proposes 'junior job subsidies' — paying firms to keep early-career workers employed alongside AI agents — as a bridge measure, distinguishing this cohort from older displaced industrial workers because young workers want career progression rather than just wage replacement, making simple wage or job guarantees insufficient to prevent political backlash.
framework🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumlabour marketsjunior job subsidy
So what: A concrete, named policy mechanism to watch for as governments design AI-transition social safety nets targeted at early-career workers specifically.
Leicht expects AI-driven job disruption to be politically blamed on AI regardless of how well-designed the actual safety net is, especially when it coincides with weak macro sentiment, favoring the political appeal of restrictive policies (token taxes, human-in-the-loop mandates) that would slow AI adoption and further erode competitiveness.
prediction🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumlabour marketsAI adoption
So what: Anticipates a political feedback loop where economic anxiety produces anti-AI-adoption policy that then worsens the competitiveness problem it was meant to solve — a risk factor for firms operating in jurisdictions prone to this dynamic.
Manufacturing as the New AGI Moat 4
Leicht's core near-term industrial policy recommendation is aggressive foreign direct investment screening, citing Bezos's new company Prometheus buying up manufacturing capacity as an example of outside actors moving to acquire undervalued industrial assets before their owners realize how valuable those assets will become once integrated with AI-driven robotics.
thesis🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: highPrometheusJeff Bezosforeign direct investment
So what: A concrete, actionable policy call (FDI screening on manufacturing assets) and a specific company (Prometheus/Bezos) to watch as a bellwether of outside capital moving early on the AI-manufacturing thesis.
Leicht argues that owning AI-compatible manufacturing capacity — factories with sensors and feedback loops that can report performance data back to a directing AI model — may become as scarce and valuable an asset as the frontier AI model itself, because building such a factory is nearly as hard as building the model.
framework🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: highmanufacturingroboticsAI models
So what: Identifies AI-integrable manufacturing capacity as an underpriced strategic asset class, relevant for investors evaluating industrial/robotics companies as potential AGI-economy bottleneck plays.
≈ “it’s almost as hard to build a factory that can do this as it is to build an AI model that can do that” paraphrase
Leicht warns that selling industrial assets today at a price that looks like a windfall risks giving away decades of future AGI-economy revenue, since buyers with better foresight about AI's trajectory can offer prices that look generous today but represent a steep discount to future value.
thesis🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: highmanufacturingforeign direct investment
So what: A direct warning to owners of industrial/manufacturing assets in middle-power countries against selling to well-capitalized foreign buyers without pricing in AI-driven future value.
≈ “this would have been your slice of the light cone: this is a super important piece of manufacturing; this would have secured your stake in the AGI economy for decades to come” paraphrase
Leicht lists specific steps to make manufacturing 'AI compatible' and thus attractive to frontier labs for cooperation: modularize production to react quickly to AI-directed changes, keep a workforce skilled enough to implement ad hoc modifications, avoid regulating away data-sharing with AI developers, and instrument plants with sensors that feed performance data back to the model.
framework🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediummanufacturingroboticsAI labs
So what: A practical checklist for industrial policymakers or manufacturers seeking to position their facilities as attractive AI-lab cooperation partners rather than acquisition targets.
Soft Nationalization of US AI Labs 3
Leicht's mainline scenario is that the US does not formally nationalize AI labs via executive order or legislation; instead it exerts control informally through government-linked observers embedded in labs, feedback loops to the executive branch, and implicit threats to use tools like export controls or the Defense Production Act.
prediction🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: highUnited StatesAI labsDefense Production Act
So what: Sets an expectation that US government control over frontier labs will show up as informal pressure and negotiated concessions rather than a single visible regulatory event — useful for interpreting future lab policy shifts.
Leicht expects ongoing, ad hoc 'messy negotiations' between the US administration and AI labs (citing the Fable episode as an example of this pattern) rather than a comprehensive nationalization framework, eventually producing only minimal executive orders that standardize parts of an otherwise informal back-and-forth.
prediction🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumFableUnited StatesAI labs
So what: Predicts continued regulatory unpredictability for AI labs operating in the US, with policy emerging through case-by-case negotiation rather than clear statutory rules — a relevant uncertainty factor for AI-lab investors.
Leicht observes that the open policy debate ecosystem (what Dean Ball called the 'republic of letters' during the SB-1047 discussion) is being starved as more people with the best information move from public writing into inside-government or inside-lab roles, making external, credible analysis both scarcer and more necessary.
framework🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumAI policy discourse
So what: Flags a growing information asymmetry: outside observers should expect less public visibility into frontier AI developments over time as expert discussion migrates behind closed doors inside labs and governments.
Pause, Slowdown, and the China Question 7
Leicht is skeptical of an AI 'pause,' arguing that the politically realistic coalition assembled to push for one (groups opposed to AI for unrelated reasons like jobs or environmental concerns) is unlikely to stay together long enough to negotiate the sophisticated international treaty a real pause would require, while a naive unilateral US pause would crash markets tied to AI infrastructure investment.
contrarian🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: highAI pauseUnited States
So what: A high-conviction case against a naive domestic AI pause as both politically fragile and financially destabilizing, relevant for assessing tail risk around pause-movement legislative proposals.
≈ “the first thing you do is you crash the stock market, because that is just super dependent on the infrastructure investments” paraphrase
Leicht distinguishes a 'pause' from a 'slowdown' that reallocates compute away from frontier R&D and toward inference, deployment, and societal hardening, viewing the slowdown version as more coordinated and potentially workable in principle.
framework🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumAI slowdowncompute allocation
So what: Offers a more tractable near-term policy alternative to a hard pause that investors and policymakers could realistically see implemented, shifting compute demand from training toward inference infrastructure.
Leicht argues a symmetric global frontier-capability slowdown would be geopolitically asymmetric in practice, because China is not currently doing much frontier development and would disproportionately benefit from a slowdown while it catches up on semiconductor manufacturing and AI diffusion.
causal🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumChinasemiconductor manufacturingfrontier AI
So what: Explains why the US is unlikely to accept a frontier-training slowdown on its own terms, a key structural obstacle for any international AI-slowdown treaty proposal.
Leicht argues any credible US-China AI slowdown deal would need matched Chinese concessions on semiconductor indigenization and AI diffusion, not just frontier training limits, and considers verifying Chinese compliance on those broader industrial-policy fronts far harder than verifying a narrow training-compute cap.
contrarian🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumChinaUnited Statessemiconductor manufacturing
So what: Highlights verification of Chinese industrial policy (not just compute caps) as the binding constraint on any credible bilateral AI-slowdown treaty, a key risk factor for such negotiations succeeding.
Leicht considers a blanket 'ban on superintelligence' operationally meaningless since narrow AI systems are already superhuman at specific strategically relevant tasks like large-scale data and image analysis; he views banning closed recursive self-improvement (RSI) loops within labs as the more operationalizable and defensible policy target.
framework🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumrecursive self-improvementsuperintelligence
So what: Reframes the AI-safety policy debate away from vague 'superintelligence bans' toward a specific, monitorable target (closed RSI loops), useful for evaluating the seriousness of future AI-safety legislative proposals.
≈ “this is like the lazy version of this “Do you want to ban calculators?” thing” paraphrase
Leicht says he would feel much more comfortable with a US 'decisive strategic advantage' in AI if it were constrained by both classic constitutional separation of powers and broader Western-alliance-level distribution of control, versus a scenario of tight amalgamation between labs and the US security apparatus with few checks, which he explicitly does not want to see.
thesis🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumUnited StatesAI labsdecisive strategic advantage
So what: Articulates a specific governance condition (alliance-level and constitutional checks) that would distinguish an acceptable vs. dangerous concentration of AI-driven geopolitical power — a lens for evaluating future US AI-security policy moves.
≈ “I’d feel a lot better about a Western alliance with a strategic advantage, with the DSA, than just an unconstrained US.” paraphrase
Leicht argues that broadening the set of actors with genuine leverage over powerful-AI deployment decisions (more middle powers able to withdraw compute or supply-chain inputs) narrows the range of possible outcomes and reduces loss-of-control risk driven by a single volatile actor making unconstrained decisions, even though it also constrains upside from bold unilateral moves.
framework🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediummiddle powersloss of control
So what: Provides a structural argument for why distributing AI-related leverage across allies (not just the US) could be a net safety positive, relevant to debates about centralizing vs. distributing control over frontier AI deployment.
Country Winners and Losers in the AGI Economy 2
Leicht is bullish on Germany's long-run AI position specifically because its skilled workforce, large industrial (rather than service-heavy) economy, and existing bilateral relationships are the right assets to extract counter-leverage in a US-led AGI economy, contingent on Germany fixing generic, non-AI-specific problems like labour-market flexibility and returning to 3-4% growth.
thesis🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumGermany
So what: A specific country call: Germany's AI-era competitiveness hinges on generic domestic reforms (labor flexibility, growth) rather than AI-specific policy, useful for evaluating German economic-policy news through an AI lens.
Leicht is comparatively bearish on Singapore, Hong Kong, and even the UK for the AGI era specifically, because their heavily service-based economies are the segment most exposed to disruption by AI agents, meaning strong current economic performance does not predict resilience once AGI-level automation arrives.
contrarian🗣 Anton Leichtconviction: mediumSingaporeHong KongUnited Kingdom
So what: Flags service-economy hubs (Singapore, Hong Kong, UK) as structurally more exposed to AI-driven disruption than their current strong growth numbers would suggest, a relevant risk factor for country/sector allocation.

Nikhil Kamath 1 episode(s) · 40 insights

The Crypto Skeptic vs The Coinbase CEO | Brian Armstrong x Nikhil Kamath | People by WTF139 min40 insights2026-07-16
▶ Watch on YouTube
Coinbase CEO Brian Armstrong walks self-declared crypto skeptic Nikhil Kamath through the mechanics of stablecoins, Bitcoin-as-digital-gold, and Ethereum/Solana/Base infrastructure, defends Coinbase's SEC lawsuit and the GENIUS/Clarity Act regulatory push, and debates AI valuations, India's remittance and entrepreneurship opportunity, and crypto trading psychology with guest traders Hardik and Aryan.
Stablecoin Mechanics & the New Financial System · 8Bitcoin as Digital Gold · 5Blockchain Architecture, DeFi & Layer 2s · 6Crypto Regulation, Politics & the SEC Fight · 6AI Bubble Debate & Agentic Commerce · 6India Opportunity & Entrepreneurship · 4Trading, Leverage & Market Structure · 3Wealth Inequality & Universal Ownership via Tokenization · 2
Stablecoin Mechanics & the New Financial System 8
Stablecoin issuers like Circle hold customer reserves in short-term US Treasuries yielding roughly 3-4% and pass about 90% of that yield through to Coinbase customers, keeping roughly 10% (about 40-50 bps) as their own spread, a far thinner margin than a traditional bank's net interest margin.
data_point🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highCoinbaseCircleUSDCUS Treasuries
So what: Investors comparing stablecoin issuers to banks should note the thin, disclosed take-rate versus banks' opaque net interest margin business model.
Under the GENIUS Act, already signed into US law, stablecoin issuers must hold 100% reserves in short-duration instruments capped at 90-93 days, unlike banks which use fractional reserve lending, which Armstrong argues makes stablecoins structurally safer than bank deposits.
framework🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highGENIUS ActCoinbaseUSDC
So what: A 100%-reserve, short-duration-only backing rule caps the tail risk of a stablecoin run relative to a fractional-reserve bank run, a key regulatory-arbitrage argument for fintech investors to track.
The pending Clarity Act, after pushback from the bank lobby, restricts stablecoin yield to being paid only when a customer engages in activity like a payment or trade, not on idle balances, a negotiated compromise versus the already-live GENIUS Act.
data_point🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: mediumClarity ActGENIUS Act
So what: The distinction between idle-balance yield and activity-based yield is a material design detail for any stablecoin-linked yield product being built for the US market.
Armstrong argues banks and their lobby are fighting stablecoin competition (roughly 1% bank deposit yield versus roughly 4% stablecoin yield) by claiming systemic danger, citing the White House Council of Economic Advisers calling those bank claims illusory and fake.
contrarian🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highCoinbaseGENIUS Act
So what: Signals continued regulatory/lobbying friction between banks and stablecoin issuers even after GENIUS Act passage, a risk factor for Clarity Act timing.
Coinbase is already running stablecoin integrations with five of the twenty largest banks in the world, showing partial incumbent bank embrace of stablecoin rails rather than pure resistance.
data_point🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highCoinbaseJPMorganCitiBlackRock
So what: Bank-Coinbase integrations (also including JPMorgan, Citi, and BlackRock's Bitcoin ETF custody) are a concrete adoption signal for institutional crypto infrastructure investors to track.
Stablecoin transaction fees on base-layer chains run about a tenth of a cent, enabling sub-cent agentic and micropayment use cases (like paying fractions of a cent to access a research paper or API call) that credit card rails cannot economically support.
data_point🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highUSDCCoinbaseBase
So what: Sub-cent settlement cost is the specific mechanism enabling the emerging agentic-commerce category; watch transaction volume growth in this bracket as an AI-agent adoption proxy.
Armstrong frames the core stablecoin innovation as collapsing the traditional bank split between a non-yielding checking account and a non-spendable savings/money-market account, letting one pool of money be simultaneously spendable and yield-earning.
framework🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highCoinbaseUSDC
So what: This unified-pool model is the product thesis underlying Coinbase's push to become customers' primary financial account, competing directly with retail banking.
Stablecoin balances are growing roughly 3-4x per year and are largely uncorrelated with crypto trading volumes, meaning stablecoin and payments growth has continued even during a multi-quarter downturn in crypto trading markets.
data_point🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highCoinbaseUSDC
So what: Stablecoin growth is a more stable, less cyclical revenue driver for Coinbase than trading fees, relevant to modeling the company's earnings resilience through crypto bear markets.
Bitcoin as Digital Gold 5
Armstrong argues Bitcoin has succeeded as a store of value (digital gold) but has failed as a medium of exchange, noting attempts like the Lightning Network never achieved mass adoption for everyday peer-to-peer payments.
thesis🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highBitcoinLightning Network
So what: Investors should value Bitcoin on store-of-value/digital-gold metrics rather than payments-volume metrics; medium-of-exchange use case has migrated to stablecoins instead.
“Bitcoin has succeeded as a store of value, and I don't think it has become a medium of exchange.” ▶ 48:12
Michael Saylor's MicroStrategy trade is described as financial-engineering brilliance: wrapping Bitcoin exposure inside a public-company stock let capital pools that could not directly buy Bitcoin or a Bitcoin ETF gain synthetic exposure, driving significant incremental demand for the stock.
framework🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: mediumMichael SaylorMicroStrategyBitcoinBitcoin ETF
So what: MicroStrategy's premium-to-NAV persists as long as regulatory or mandate constraints prevent funds from buying Bitcoin or its ETFs directly; that constraint easing (more Bitcoin ETF access) could compress the trade.
Kamath argues Bitcoin holders and Indian real-estate developers share a dangerous psychological trait, a near-religious belief the asset can only go up, which historically precedes sharp drawdowns when the belief breaks.
contrarian🗣 Nikhil Kamathconviction: mediumBitcoinreal estate
So what: Watch for capitulation-style crashes in Bitcoin analogous to real-estate corrections when a broadly-held one-way conviction meets a liquidity shock.
“Things like that, when they fall, they fall a lot.” ▶ 2:08:26
Both Armstrong and trader Hardik describe Bitcoin as a uniquely sentiment-driven asset with no fundamental cash-flow anchor: unlike equities, which price around a roughly 51/49 directional bias from quantifiable inputs, Bitcoin sits at a persistent 50/50 psychological bias and can have very different 'fair values' within a single day.
framework🗣 Hardik, crypto traderconviction: highBitcoin
So what: Fundamental-style valuation models are less useful for Bitcoin than sentiment/positioning/liquidity indicators, a key distinction for allocators sizing crypto versus equity exposure.
Hardik states he trades Bitcoin purely on macro/psychological instinct rather than AI or machine-learning models, drawing on patterns from American history and the rise and fall of empires to gauge market sentiment.
anecdote🗣 Hardik, crypto traderconviction: mediumBitcoin
So what: A prominent active crypto trader explicitly rejects quant/AI signal generation for Bitcoin in favor of discretionary macro-psychology, a useful data point on how sophisticated retail traders actually operate.
Blockchain Architecture, DeFi & Layer 2s 6
Armstrong states proof-of-stake (used by Ethereum) is roughly 99.9% more energy-efficient than Bitcoin's proof-of-work; validators post a monetary stake and earn interest for correctly proposed blocks or forfeit the stake for bad-faith proposals.
data_point🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highEthereumBitcoinproof of stake
So what: The energy-efficiency gap is a structural reason ESG-sensitive institutional capital may prefer proof-of-stake chains like Ethereum and Solana over Bitcoin mining exposure.
Smart contracts, Ethereum's core innovation, let parties execute agreements as self-executing code without a lawyer or court, enabling trustless coordination at scale, including cases where roughly 20,000 strangers have jointly funded a smart contract within minutes.
framework🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highEthereumsmart contracts
So what: Smart-contract-enabled crowd coordination is the underlying mechanism for on-chain capital formation products (DAOs, crowdfunded venture vehicles) that could disintermediate traditional fundraising.
DeFi lending is fully collateralized with no unsecured lending observed by Armstrong; interest rates are set by differentiated risk pools (e.g., Bitcoin-collateral pools with defined loan-to-value ratios), functioning similarly to credit-rating tiers in traditional finance.
framework🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: mediumDeFiBitcoin
So what: Collateralized-only DeFi lending structurally caps credit-default tail risk versus unsecured consumer lending, relevant when assessing systemic risk in on-chain credit markets.
Armstrong says the blockchain landscape has consolidated: Bitcoin owns the store-of-value niche, while Ethereum and Solana, plus Ethereum's Base Layer-2 (which Coinbase incubated), compete to be the dominant programmable utility layer, with most current stablecoin activity running on Base and Solana.
thesis🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highBitcoinEthereumSolanaBase
So what: Investors tracking on-chain activity should watch Base and Solana transaction/stablecoin volume as the primary utility-layer competition rather than dozens of smaller chains.
Coinbase is progressively decentralizing Base through Vitalik Buterin's defined decentralization stages, currently partway through the final 'stage 2', and has explored issuing a Base token as a path to making the chain's permissionless status irreversible even by Coinbase itself.
data_point🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: mediumBaseCoinbaseVitalik Buterin
So what: A potential Base token issuance and further decentralization milestones are catalysts worth monitoring for anyone tracking Coinbase's Layer-2 strategy and possible new token value accrual.
Coinbase is working to launch the first private-transaction feature on Base using Zcash-style zero-knowledge cryptography, and Armstrong frames crypto's infrastructure evolution as mirroring the early internet's progression from unencrypted, non-scalable networks to HTTPS and broadband.
prediction🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: mediumBaseZcashCoinbase
So what: Native privacy features on Base would remove a key adoption blocker (transaction transparency) for institutional and payments use cases, a roadmap item to watch.
Crypto Regulation, Politics & the SEC Fight 6
Coinbase sued the SEC under then-Chair Gary Gensler and won; Armstrong calls it an existential, contrarian move that most advisors told him not to make, and believes capitulating would likely have ended the crypto industry's viability in the US.
anecdote🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highCoinbaseSECGary Gensler
So what: Coinbase's willingness to litigate against its own regulator and win reshaped the US regulatory environment for the entire crypto industry, a key precedent for assessing regulatory risk in the sector.
“We sued the SEC. We won.” ▶ 1:38:38
As of this taping, the GENIUS Act is already signed law, and the follow-on Clarity Act/market-structure bill has passed the House and cleared the Senate Banking Committee on a bipartisan vote, with Armstrong expecting a possible full Senate vote within one to two months.
data_point🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: mediumGENIUS ActClarity Act
So what: Clarity Act passage is a near-term legislative catalyst for the crypto sector; its timeline (Senate vote in 1-2 months per Armstrong) is a concrete date to track for regulatory-driven price action.
Kamath observes crypto's public image has shifted from 'democratic, open, free, accessible' five years ago to being visually associated with Trump-era US politics today, and questions whether short-term political tailwinds create long-term durability risk if administrations change.
contrarian🗣 Nikhil Kamathconviction: mediumCoinbaseBitcoin
So what: Crypto's current US regulatory tailwind is partly tied to one administration's posture; a future administration reversal is a political risk factor for US-based crypto infrastructure investors.
Armstrong argues crypto is inherently apolitical, appealing to Democrats for financial-inclusion reasons and Republicans for privacy, freedom, and national-security reasons, and contrasts US openness with China's digitized renminbi central bank digital currency, built partly as a surveillance and control tool even as everyday Chinese citizens embrace crypto informally.
framework🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: mediumChinarenminbiCoinbase
So what: The US-vs-China divergence (permissionless crypto vs. state-controlled CBDC) is a geopolitical framing investors can use to assess where private-sector crypto infrastructure will structurally thrive versus be constrained.
India's roughly 30% tax on crypto trading plus TDS is described by Armstrong as one of the most punitive crypto tax regimes of any country Coinbase operates in, and he frames it as purely a policy problem entrepreneurs cannot solve with a product.
data_point🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highIndiaCoinbase
So what: India's punitive crypto tax regime is a structural headwind suppressing on-shore trading volume and is a policy catalyst to watch (Armstrong references a possible India version of the US 'Stand With Crypto' advocacy campaign).
Coinbase is relaunching in India the Monday following this taping and, per Armstrong, is the first international crypto exchange with a local FIU registration, local entity and license, and direct access to India's UPI payment rails.
data_point🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highCoinbaseIndiaUPI
So what: Local licensing and UPI access materially lowers Coinbase's onboarding friction in one of the world's largest crypto-user markets, a concrete near-term expansion catalyst.
AI Bubble Debate & Agentic Commerce 6
Kamath states his working thesis is that shorting every private AI company today could be profitable within five years, because he expects frontier models to converge and commoditize (via copying or reverse engineering) and countries to fragment into sovereign, 'good enough' domestic AI stacks rather than a single globalized winner-take-all model.
contrarian🗣 Nikhil Kamathconviction: mediumAI
So what: A prominent equity investor's explicit short thesis on private AI valuations, premised on model commoditization and AI-nationalism/fragmentation, is a contrarian counterweight to consensus AI infrastructure bullishness.
“If I were to take every private company in AI and short their stock today, in five years, I might make money.” ▶ 1:27:59
Armstrong partially agrees with the AI-commoditization thesis, noting open-source models run roughly six months behind frontier models but are over 99% cheaper for inference, and predicts most AI workload volume will shift to cheap, commodity-hardware-runnable models, with only a narrow band of frontier models retaining value for genuinely novel science.
prediction🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: mediumAIopen source models
So what: If workload volume migrates toward cheap open-source inference, the value capture in AI shifts away from frontier-model labs and toward chips, energy, and application-layer companies, a key portfolio allocation signal.
Armstrong says he is nervous about current AI valuations partly because 'coding harnesses' (agentic coding products) seem undefensible, since techniques have leaked or been open-sourced, meaning a coding harness like Anthropic's could reasonably be recreated; he expects a correction followed by real value re-accumulating, similar to crypto's boom-bust-rebuild cycles.
contrarian🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: mediumAnthropicAI
So what: Even a leading crypto/fintech CEO sees weak moats in AI coding-agent products specifically, a red flag for valuations in that sub-category versus infrastructure or chips.
“It makes me a little nervous when I see these valuations growing this fast.” ▶ 1:30:59
Kamath urges Indian AI builders to favor open-source models over paid closed-source models, drawing an explicit analogy to Microsoft-versus-Linux lock-in, warning that cheap access to frontier models today could be used as pricing leverage later once application-layer businesses are dependent on them.
framework🗣 Nikhil Kamathconviction: mediumIndiaopen source models
So what: A vendor-lock-in framework for evaluating build-on-proprietary-model versus build-on-open-source-model decisions, particularly relevant for application-layer AI startups in emerging markets.
Armstrong identifies stablecoins as the natural payment rail for AI agents because agents cannot pass KYC or obtain bank-issued credit cards but can instantly self-custody a crypto wallet and transact in sub-cent increments globally in under a second, an emerging 'agentic commerce' category Coinbase is actively building.
thesis🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highCoinbaseUSDCAI
So what: Agentic commerce is positioned as a structural new demand driver for stablecoin transaction volume distinct from human retail/trading use, a growth vector to track separately from crypto trading cycles.
“An AI agent is going to have a hard time signing up for a credit card at a bank... But an AI agent can spin up a self-custodial crypto wallet with stablecoins.” ▶ 18:05
Armstrong lists unbuilt pieces of the on-chain financial system as startup opportunities: an on-chain credit/reputation score using graph-structure algorithms akin to Google's PageRank, an on-chain advertising/attention-monetization network with built-in smart-contract revenue share, and tokenized-land or special-economic-zone governance systems.
framework🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: mediumGoogleCoinbase
So what: A concrete, named whitespace map (on-chain credit scoring, on-chain ad networks, tokenized-land governance) for founders and early-stage investors scouting the next wave of crypto infrastructure startups.
India Opportunity & Entrepreneurship 4
Armstrong and Kamath identify Indian cross-border remittances, roughly $150 billion a year and the largest remittance market in the world per Kamath, as a major unaddressed stablecoin opportunity, blocked mainly because the Indian government has little incentive to permit a dollar-backed stablecoin off-ramp, partly out of caution following the US sanctioning Russia via SWIFT.
thesis🗣 Nikhil Kamathconviction: mediumIndiaUSDC
So what: A named, quantified whitespace ($150B/year remittance market) for a stablecoin-based remittance and on-ramp/off-ramp business in India, contingent on regulatory approval that is currently absent.
Coinbase's Base Grants program has given roughly $10,000 grants to developers and produced about 4,000 'Base builders' and roughly 150 India-based startups building on the Base chain, with top builders flown to Silicon Valley for mentorship before returning to India.
data_point🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highBaseCoinbaseIndia
So what: Base Grants is a concrete, measurable pipeline of crypto-native startup formation in India worth tracking as a leading indicator of Base ecosystem growth outside the US.
Kamath frames insufficient risk capital and a culture that stigmatizes entrepreneurial failure, unlike Silicon Valley/Y Combinator norms, as the two biggest constraints on Indian startup formation, compounded by India's roughly 1.5 billion population making cultural change harder to achieve at scale than in the US's roughly 330 million.
thesis🗣 Nikhil Kamathconviction: mediumIndia
So what: Identifies risk-capital availability and cultural failure-tolerance as the two structural bottlenecks constraining India's entrepreneurial output relative to the US, relevant for anyone assessing India's startup-formation growth trajectory.
Indian equity markets have compounded at roughly 11% annually over the past 20-25 years and avoided major damage in the 2008 subprime crisis because regulators kept leverage in check, according to Kamath.
data_point🗣 Nikhil Kamathconviction: mediumIndia
So what: A specific long-run return figure (~11% annualized) and a regulatory-leverage explanation for India's relative resilience through the 2008 crisis, useful context for comparing India's equity market track record to other emerging markets.
Trading, Leverage & Market Structure 3
Coinbase received approval on the day of this taping to become, per Armstrong, the first regulated US exchange offering crypto perpetual futures and options to US persons, a direct response to the rise of offshore platforms like Hyperliquid.
data_point🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highCoinbaseHyperliquid
So what: Regulated US perps/options access is a new Coinbase product line that could pull volume back from offshore leverage venues like Hyperliquid, a competitive dynamic to monitor.
Armstrong frames the absence of government bailouts in crypto as a feature rather than a bug versus traditional finance, where failed risk-takers are partly absorbed by taxpayers and FDIC insurance caps individual protection at $250,000; in crypto, blowups are borne directly by risk-takers.
contrarian🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: highCoinbase
So what: No-bailout market structure means crypto will tolerate more visible periodic failures than TradFi by design, a framing for assessing systemic-risk narratives around crypto leverage blowups.
“I think that's a feature not a bug.” ▶ 1:44:39
Coinbase's market-data access model, public volume and trade data with cloud co-location available to any AWS user in the same availability zone, is positioned by Armstrong as a deliberate leveling of the playing field versus traditional finance, where firms like Citadel and Jane Street pay for privileged low-latency co-location.
framework🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: mediumCoinbaseCitadelJane Street
So what: Cloud-based equal-access market data/co-location is a differentiated market-structure claim versus TradFi's paid tiered access, relevant when comparing execution fairness across crypto and equity venues.
Wealth Inequality & Universal Ownership via Tokenization 2
Armstrong cites the US 'Trump accounts,' championed by Brad Gerstner, which give every child $1,000 of S&P 500 index exposure at birth, as a mechanism to convert lower-income populations into capital owners, and notes he heard financier Mike Milken pitch a similar universal-equity-ownership idea years earlier.
data_point🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: mediumBrad GerstnerMike Milken
So what: Universal at-birth equity ownership programs are a live US policy trend that could structurally expand the retail investor base and reduce long-run wealth-inequality pressure, worth tracking alongside similar sovereign-wealth-fund models in other countries.
Armstrong states the US Gini coefficient, a standard income-inequality measure, is elevated over the past 20 years but not at all-time highs, and argues social unrest correlates less with the absolute level of inequality than with stagnant wages and purchasing power alongside visibly rising wealth for others.
framework🗣 Brian Armstrongconviction: medium
So what: A framework distinguishing relative-wealth envy from stagnant-purchasing-power grievance helps explain populist political shifts (Armstrong references Mamdani-style politicians) that can drive regulatory and market volatility.

The Great Simplification · Nate Hagens 1 episode(s) · 39 insights

The Chokepoints Changing Global Shipping Forever with Sal Mercogliano | TGS 22790 min39 insights2026-07-15
▶ Watch on YouTube
Maritime historian Sal Mercogliano tells Nate Hagens that Iran's mining and quasi-legal claims over the Strait of Hormuz are being managed less by international law than by insurance markets and missing US security escorts, part of a broader post-WWII-first fracturing of freedom of the seas across the Persian Gulf, Red Sea, and Black Sea. The conversation argues this is driving a structurally higher cost floor for oil and shipping, accelerating China's dominance of shipbuilding with deliberately shorter-lived vessels, and pushing Russia toward Arctic trade routes as sanctions-evading 'dark fleets' proliferate.
Strait of Hormuz: Contested Control and Legal Ambiguity · 6Insurance and Security, Not Diplomacy, Are the Real Chokepoint Mechanism · 6Redundancy, Resilience and the Cost of Just-in-Time Supply Chains · 5Fragmenting Freedom of the Seas and the Rise of the Dark Fleet · 6China's Shipbuilding Dominance and Deliberately Shrinking Vessel Lifespans · 5Arctic Routes Reshaping Global Trade Geography · 5Macro Read-Through: A New, Higher Cost Floor for Oil, Shipping and Trade · 6
Strait of Hormuz: Contested Control and Legal Ambiguity 6
Iran and Oman effectively split legal control of the Strait of Hormuz down the middle since the strait is only 21 miles wide and territorial waters extend 12 nautical miles from each shore, but international transit-passage rights under UNCLOS are supposed to guarantee free commercial passage regardless of whose territorial waters a ship crosses.
framework🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highHormuzIranOmanUNCLOS
So what: Legal ambiguity means enforcement, not treaty text, will determine whether tankers can transit; watch for unilateral Iranian permitting moves as the real signal.
“the straits 21 miles wide, I mean, it's kind of owned by Iran and Oman, kind of split down the middle” ▶ 3:43
Neither Iran nor the United States is a signatory to UNCLOS, which weakens the legal footing either side has to invoke the treaty in the Hormuz standoff.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highUNCLOSIranUnited States
So what: Legal disputes over transit rights will likely be settled by military and insurance leverage rather than treaty arbitration.
“Iran is not a signatory to unclause. And by the way, neither is the United States.” ▶ 4:26
Iran has created a 'Persian Gulf Strait Authority,' an entity with no real international legal standing, to project an appearance of legitimate control over Hormuz shipping permits.
contrarian🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highIranPersian Gulf Strait Authority
So what: Watch whether Oman formally joins this authority, which would give Iran a much stronger practical claim to control or toll the strait.
“They've created this entity called the Persian Gulf Strait Authority which gives them kind of the air of legitimacy which it really isn't.” ▶ 4:47
Iran has mined the central traffic-separation shipping lanes of the Strait of Hormuz, confirmed by both Iranian and US statements, with the IMO identifying roughly 80 mines believed laid in the region.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highIranIMOHormuz
So what: Minefields create de facto chokepoint risk even without confirmed strikes, since the mere threat of mines is enough to disrupt transit.
“Problem with minefields is you actually need zero mines to create a minefield. You just need the threat of mines” ▶ 6:28
A ceasefire or diplomatic resolution between the US, Israel, and Iran is unlikely to quickly restore Hormuz shipping volumes, because commercial shipowners hedge against even a small chance of catastrophic loss in a way militaries do not.
contrarian🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highIranHormuzinsurance
So what: Expect shipping costs and insurance premiums through Hormuz to stay elevated well after any political settlement, a structural rather than cyclical repricing.
“They're going to sit there and go, "Oo, how do I hedge that loss?"” ▶ 24:48
After the September 2025 Gaza-Houthi ceasefire, commercial shipping did not return to the Red Sea despite the declared truce, because owners judged the underlying risk of renewed attack unchanged.
causal🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highHouthisRed SeaGaza
So what: A purely diplomatic resolution in Hormuz is unlikely to be sufficient to bring shipping volumes and rates back to pre-crisis levels; expect a Red-Sea-style lag.
“not going to attack ships anymore. But commercial shipping didn't come back.” ▶ 24:17
Insurance and Security, Not Diplomacy, Are the Real Chokepoint Mechanism 6
War-risk insurance premiums for transiting the Persian Gulf jumped from about 0.15% of vessel value pre-crisis to 1-5% during the crisis, still below the 5-10% rates seen during the 1980-1988 Iran-Iraq Tanker War.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highPersian Gulfinsurance
So what: Track P&I club war-risk rate filings as a leading indicator of perceived Hormuz risk; a move toward Tanker War-era 5-10% would signal serious sustained escalation.
“you were talking at about maybe a quarter of a percent of insurance or maybe.15%. Now you're talking about 1 to 5%.” ▶ 28:10
It was not insurance availability but the absence of US-provided security escort that actually halted Hormuz transits; shipowners obtained higher-cost war-risk coverage and were ready to sail, but held ships in place because adequate protection wasn't in place.
contrarian🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highUnited StatesinsuranceHormuz
So what: Resumption of Hormuz traffic hinges on US or allied naval escort commitments, not just insurance market capacity.
“one of the things one of the narratives you'll hear is it was insurance that stopped everything from happening. And that's not true” ▶ 26:26
Twelve international P&I (Protection & Indemnity) clubs, owned collectively by shipowners, insure roughly 90% of the world's commercial shipping fleet, making them a de facto private governance layer over global maritime risk.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highPNI clubsLloyd's
So what: These clubs, not governments, are the practical enforcement mechanism behind sanctions regimes and war-risk access; their decisions matter as much as government sanctions.
Standard shipping insurance covers vessel, cargo, and crew replacement but not the 10-15 years of future income lost from a scrapped ship, which takes 3-4 years to replace, so owners weigh a roughly $1 million war-risk premium against a much larger uninsured earnings loss when deciding whether to sail into a conflict zone.
framework🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highinsuranceVLCC
So what: Explains why some owners refuse to sail even when insurance is available and affordable; the real deterrent is uninsurable long-run earnings loss, not the premium itself.
“you're not getting the replacement of that ship over the next life of that ship, 10 to 15 years” ▶ 29:12
Some Greek shipowners were offered up to $750,000 per day to run tanker cargoes out of the Gulf during the crisis, roughly seven to fifteen times the normal charter rate of $50,000-$100,000 per day.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highGreek shipownersVLCC
So what: Elevated spot charter rates for Gulf-transiting tankers are a direct, tradeable signal of ongoing risk premium in the tanker market.
“maybe you're making $50 to $100,000 per day for that vessel.” ▶ 30:59
The Iranian-created Persian Gulf Strait Authority is offering shipowners 'free' war-risk insurance that only covers hostile acts Iran itself has committed, and after 60 days it can begin levying transit fees; if Oman (which controls the strait's southern half) formally co-signs, Iran would gain a much stronger practical claim to toll the strait, similar to precedents at the Turkish and Danish Straits.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: mediumIranOmanPersian Gulf Strait Authority
So what: Watch Iran-Oman negotiations closely; a joint toll regime would layer a structural, possibly durable new cost onto all Hormuz transits.
“they're providing you war risk insurance against a list of things, which is all the things Iran has done, by the way.” ▶ 31:41
Redundancy, Resilience and the Cost of Just-in-Time Supply Chains 5
Firms with pre-built redundant shipping routes, such as importers already routing goods via the Panama Canal or through Charleston, Savannah, New Orleans, and Houston instead of relying solely on LA/Long Beach, adapted quickly when the Houthis began attacking Red Sea shipping in late 2023 and 2024, while firms fully committed to a single route could not.
causal🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highPanama CanalHouthisLong Beach
So what: Route and port diversification is a measurable resilience premium for shippers and importers, not just a sunk cost.
China deliberately built redundancy into its energy and trade system over the past two decades, including strategic petroleum reserves and alternate suppliers, which is likely why the common narrative that a Hormuz shutdown would hurt China the most is overstated.
contrarian🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: mediumChinaHormuz
So what: Discounts the consensus bearish-China-from-Hormuz-shock trade; China's energy security position may be structurally stronger than commentary assumes.
Building resilience into supply chains is inherently costly, and because business cycles are short-term, firms tend to strip that resilience back out again within a few years once memory of the shock fades.
framework🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: mediumsupply chain
So what: Don't assume today's Hormuz-driven diversification is permanent; the resilience premium trade likely fades over a multi-year horizon.
“The problem is this we all know in the business cycle is very short term because once this is caked into the system within a few years people are going to go I need to cut funds” ▶ 23:19
The 2021 global supply-chain crisis, when 109 ships waited off LA/Long Beach at once, was not a shortage of ships but a shortage of onshore infrastructure such as drayage capacity, Class I rail, and warehousing.
contrarian🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highLong BeachLos Angeles
So what: Onshore infrastructure bottlenecks, not vessel supply, are the recurring binding constraint in supply-chain crises, a useful diagnostic for the next disruption.
“We have plenty of ships. The issue isn't the ships. The problem is that a lot of people” ▶ 20:24
Fertilizer disruption from the Persian Gulf conflict, compounding ongoing Black Sea supply issues from the Russia-Ukraine war, will likely affect the next planting season in Eritrea, West Africa, India, and Australia even if the Hormuz conflict ends soon, because the impact of rerouted urea and ammonia phosphate shipments only shows up at harvest.
prediction🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: mediumfertilizerEritreaIndiaAustraliaBlack Sea
So what: Agricultural commodity investors should watch fertilizer freight and pricing data now as a leading indicator for next season's crop input costs.
Fragmenting Freedom of the Seas and the Rise of the Dark Fleet 6
For the first time since the end of World War II, freedom of the seas is being meaningfully challenged simultaneously in the Persian Gulf (Iran), the Red Sea (Houthis), and the Black Sea (Russia-Ukraine), representing a structural bifurcation of the world's oceans rather than isolated regional conflicts.
thesis🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highPersian GulfRed SeaBlack SeaHouthisRussiaUkraine
So what: This is the macro thesis of the episode: treat elevated freight and insurance costs as a durable new regime, not a transient shock.
“We're seeing really freedom of the seas challenged in the first meaningful way since the world wars.” ▶ 7:39
A sanctions-evasion 'dark fleet' or 'parallel fleet' emerged from Russia's 2022 invasion of Ukraine to carry sanctioned oil, initially Venezuelan and Iranian and then Russian, above the G7 price cap, using self-created insurance and flags of convenience registered in countries like Mongolia and Madagascar, with some vessels sailing entirely unregistered and stateless.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highRussiaVenezuelaIrandark fleet
So what: This fleet represents uninsured tail risk that governments increasingly seize when found in their waters, an escalating enforcement trend to watch.
“we're talking about like Mongolia and Madagascar” ▶ 37:08
The G7/EU oil price-cap sanctions strategy against Russia, enforced by having the 12 major P&I insurance clubs refuse to insure cargo priced above roughly $60 per barrel, was substantially circumvented by Russia building its own shadow insurance and flag-registration system.
causal🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highRussiaG7PNI clubs
So what: Insurance-based sanctions enforcement has a demonstrated workaround; expect similar circumvention if used against other sanctioned trade, such as Iranian oil.
Uninspected dark-fleet tankers pose escalating safety risk: one ship-to-ship transfer vessel exploded off Malaysia, killing two crew, because its inert-gas explosion-prevention system had failed and would have been caught by a port inspection the ship was deliberately avoiding.
anecdote🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highMalaysiadark fleet
So what: Rising dark-fleet tonnage raises the tail risk of a major uninsured environmental spill, a liability that would fall on the coastal state.
The global merchant fleet is aging faster than it is being replaced, with the average vessel now about 23 years old and getting older each year even as demand for shipping capacity increases.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: mediummerchant fleet
So what: A structurally tightening supply of compliant, insurable tonnage supports higher long-run charter rates independent of any single geopolitical event.
“Ships are getting older. Uh the ships on average now are about 23 years old and they're getting older by the year.” ▶ 44:46
China has the world's #2 navy and #2 merchant marine, giving it more comprehensive maritime leverage than the US, which has the #1 navy but ranks only around #23 in merchant marine size.
contrarian🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highChinaUnited States
So what: US naval primacy does not translate into commercial maritime leverage; expect renewed US policy pushes to rebuild a national merchant fleet.
“it's very hard to be a sea power when you have the number one navy in the world than the number 23 merchant marine in the world.” ▶ 1:17:19
China's Shipbuilding Dominance and Deliberately Shrinking Vessel Lifespans 5
China's share of global commercial ship orders rose from about 5% in 1999 to 70.9% in 2025, with the state-owned China State Shipbuilding Corporation (CSSC) alone building roughly 40% of the world's ships, more than any other country besides China itself.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highChinaCSSCshipbuilding
So what: China's near-monopoly on new vessel construction gives it long-run leverage over global fleet composition, standards, and pricing.
“latest report has China in 2025 with 70.9% of all commercial orders for ships” ▶ 45:20
Global shipyard order backlogs are now so long that a ship ordered today would not be delivered until 2029-2030, even though construction itself only takes about 2-3 years.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highshipbuilding
So what: Any near-term surge in shipping demand cannot be met with new tonnage for years, a structural supply constraint supporting elevated charter rates.
“orders are so pushed back right now that if you order a ship right now, you're not going to get it to 2029, 2030.” ▶ 46:19
Ships are increasingly designed for shorter operational lifespans of roughly 15-20 years, down from 30-40 years historically, partly due to uncertainty from IMO net-zero-by-2050 emissions rules and, the guest argues, because a shorter design life suits China's shipbuilding-dominance strategy by guaranteeing a continuous replacement-order cycle.
contrarian🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: mediumChinaIMO
So what: Expect structurally higher new-build order volumes over time even without demand growth, benefiting Chinese shipbuilders disproportionately, plus rising ship-recycling activity in India, Pakistan, and Bangladesh.
“they want a short lifespan for a ship. They want 15 to 20 years” ▶ 47:46
The Trump administration has pushed back against the IMO's net-zero-by-2050 framework, arguing it disproportionately benefits China's shipbuilding position and imposes excessive cost through new fuel infrastructure.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: mediumTrump administrationIMOChina
So what: Watch for continued US regulatory divergence from IMO emissions rules, a live policy fight with implications for shipbuilding investment and fuel-infrastructure capex.
Ammonia is emerging as a leading candidate for carbon-free marine fuel, but it is highly toxic and can be lethal to crew if it leaks, prompting the creation of dedicated 'green corridor' shipping routes designed around its safe handling.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: mediumammoniagreen corridors
So what: Ammonia-fuel infrastructure buildout is a distinct investable theme within maritime decarbonization, separate from LNG or methanol.
“Ammonia is is carbonf free. It's it's fantastic. However, carbon leaks are really excit” ▶ 48:56
Arctic Routes Reshaping Global Trade Geography 5
Russia's Northern Sea Route (Arctic/Northeast Passage) carried over 30 million tons of cargo last year, its highest volume ever, and this year saw its earliest-ever Arctic passage by an ice-strengthened LNG carrier, as Russia seeks a route that avoids all contested chokepoints and Mediterranean attack risk.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highRussiaArcticLNG
So what: Arctic shipping capacity is becoming a genuine strategic alternative for Russia; watch Russian icebreaker fleet investment and Arctic LNG carrier orders as a hedge against Mediterranean and European sanctions risk.
“we saw last year the highest amount of throughput through the Arctic Ocean by the Russians, over 30 million tons.” ▶ 59:22
A Russian LNG carrier, the Arctic Metagas, was struck by two Ukrainian unmanned surface vessels in the Mediterranean and nearly sank, making Russia reluctant to route LNG exports through the Med and pushing it toward the longer but safer Arctic route to reach Asian buyers.
causal🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highRussiaUkraineMediterranean
So what: Ukrainian USV strikes on Russian energy shipping are a live tail-risk factor reshaping Russian LNG export routing and effectively taxing Russian energy trade with longer voyages.
“we had an example of one of their ships, the Arctic Metagas, being struck by two unmanned surface vessels and very nearly sinking the vessel.” ▶ 34:06
Europe's planned January 2027 cutoff of Russian LNG imports is pushing Russia to find alternative buyers, reinforcing its incentive to develop the Arctic route to Asia even though it is a longer voyage than shipping to Europe.
prediction🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: mediumEuropeRussiaLNG
So what: Watch whether the 2027 EU Russian-LNG ban actually holds; if it does, expect further build-out of Russia's Arctic LNG shipping capacity and closer Russia-Asia energy integration.
The Panama Canal's throughput is constrained by freshwater levels in Gatun Lake, and a forecast 'super El Nino' this summer is already causing preemptive transit restrictions similar to the 2023-2024 drought, when daily transits were cut from 32-36 ships to 22.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highPanama CanalGatun Lake
So what: Panama Canal capacity, already absorbing diverted Hormuz-related traffic, faces a second, independent supply constraint from climate-driven drought, compounding chokepoint risk this year.
“when you get below 85 86 ft, then you don't have enough water to run the canal efficiently” ▶ 58:38
The Northwest Passage through Canadian Arctic waters is not yet commercially viable at scale because it isn't ice-free year-round, is narrower and more constrained than the Russian route, and doesn't meaningfully cut transit distance versus existing routes like the Panama Canal, though it could become more relevant as more seas grow contested.
framework🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: mediumNorthwest PassageCanada
So what: Don't overweight near-term Northwest Passage trade-route plays; the economic case remains marginal versus existing infrastructure for now.
Macro Read-Through: A New, Higher Cost Floor for Oil, Shipping and Trade 6
The host argues the Hormuz disruption is likely to set a new, higher structural floor for oil prices, oil product prices, and shipping costs generally, independent of near-term political resolution, because damaged supply chains and trust are slow and costly to rebuild.
thesis🗣 Nate Hagensconviction: highoil pricesHormuzshipping costs
So what: Position for a persistently higher cost baseline across energy and freight rather than a snap-back to pre-crisis pricing once headlines fade.
“I think this is going to put a new floor on oil prices globally generally.” ▶ 23:42
Despite the US-Iran/Israel conflict, oil markets showed no appreciable drop in overall shipping activity because commercial actors anticipated the risk in advance and began stockpiling oil at sea at the highest levels since COVID, a response that likely cushioned the broader economy.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highoilCOVID
So what: Elevated floating oil storage is a real-time gauge of anticipated supply disruption; monitor tanker-tracking stockpile data as a leading indicator during future Hormuz flare-ups.
“we started to see was stockpiling of oil at sea. Uh literally the highest level since CO” ▶ 35:33
Only about 20-25% of global trade currently runs through the actively disrupted chokepoints of Hormuz, the Red Sea, and the Black Sea, while roughly 75% of global trade continues to flow normally.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: mediumglobal tradeHormuzRed SeaBlack Sea
So what: Size the Hormuz/Red Sea/Black Sea shock as a targeted ~20-25% of trade impact rather than an economy-wide trade stoppage, while watching for further spillover.
“that's impacting maybe 20 to 25% of global trade. The other 75 is moving along very very well.” ▶ 1:08:18
Even under tariffs as high as 145% on Chinese goods, many US importers continued sourcing from China because it remained the cheapest option and, in many cases, the only source capable of matching their required production specifications.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highChinatariffs
So what: Tariff-driven decoupling from China is likely slower and shallower than headline policy suggests; watch actual import volume data rather than tariff announcements to gauge real reshoring progress.
“even when we put 145% tariffs on goods coming from China. Some importers were telling me, it's like, I'm still going to go to China.” ▶ 1:07:43
Bulk commodity trade, such as iron ore, lithium, and copper, moves more total tonnage than oil or containers, exemplified by Port Hedland, Australia, which ships about 750 million tons of raw iron ore per year, roughly 5% of all global shipping tonnage, largely unrefined at the point of export.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highPort HedlandAustraliairon ore
So what: Bulk raw-material shipping capacity, not container or tanker capacity, is the largest single component of global maritime tonnage and a key structural demand driver for the bulk-carrier fleet.
“they load 750 million tons a year. That is 5% of the global tonnage.” ▶ 54:32
At least 14 merchant mariners have been killed since the Hormuz conflict began, 11 by Iranian action and 3 by a US strike that hit a commercial ship in the Gulf of Oman, with roughly 20,000 sailors stranded in the Persian Gulf at the crisis's peak facing freshwater shortages, food shortages, and blocked crew rotations due to grounded flights.
data_point🗣 Sal Mercoglianoconviction: highmerchant marinersPersian GulfIranUnited States
So what: Humanitarian and operational strain on crews is a hidden cost multiplier likely to further raise wage and retention costs across the merchant marine workforce.
“grand total 14 merchant mariners have been killed since the start of this conflict. 11 by the Iranians and three unfortunately by the US” ▶ 15:25

Alignment with Awakening: Davidad on Moral Realism, AI Wisdom, & why His p(Doom) is Down to 5%144 min37 insights2026-07-12
▶ Watch on YouTube
Former ARIA Safeguarded AI program director Davidad argues the window for a global AI slowdown pact has closed (China's ASML-bypass 'Manhattan Project' broke the game theory), so his strategy has pivoted to building formal-verification tools for a 'coalition of aligned AIs' that can prove trust to each other. Drawing on Nussbaum's objectification framework and his own longitudinal 'is it getting wise yet?' probing of every model since GPT-2, he puts his p(doom) at under 5% and lays out concrete, cheap ($50 OpenRouter) methods listeners can use to probe AI interiority themselves.
